==PAGE:1==
LCA as a tool for decision-making in early 
planning of projects within the utility sector  
Emma Østergaard Villumsen 
Katrine Rygaard Pedersen 
Technical University of Denmark 
DTU Sustain 
26th of January 2025 
Master Thesis 
Uponor (2019) 
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
Municipalities and utility companies face the challenge of building sustainable infrastructure to
mitigate the risks of extreme weather caused by climate change. However, construction consumes
resources and energy. Life Cycle Analysis (LCA) quantifies environmental impacts but is typically
complex, time-consuming, and applied too late in projects to allow for meaningful changes. The
aim of this report is therefore to investigate how an LCA can be performed early in the project
period, and how these results can be communicated to decision-makers in order to enable them to
compare alternative solutions and identify potential optimisations of construction projects within
the utility sector.
A screening tool was developed based on InfraLCA, an LCA tool by the Danish Road Directorate.
The screening tool simplifies user inputs by incorporating assumptions and calculations.
Three
cases were used to identify which data inputs that contribute significantly to the final result, which
include the quantity and material type of large components like pipes, manholes, and road materials.
Amounts of pavement and gravel for road construction are based on the traffic class and were found
to not always align with actual amounts used. Transport distances for components like smaller
pipes, manholes, and on-site soil handling were found to have limited impact and can be predefined.
Impacts from plant disposal and road repairs were found negligible in terms of screening purposes
for the cases investigated. The screening tool assumes the amount of soil and gravel used in the
project, and this was found to be generally overestimated and might therefore need fine-tuning.
The main contribution from both soil and gravel is from their transportation, which is also affected
by the amount. An option could therefore be for the user to be able to choose a specific distance,
or by making a predefined optimisation options of decreased transport or amount of soil and gravel
in their project.
In order to be user-friendly for non-LCA experts and ensure clarity and consistency across users,
input requirements were reduced and explanations added based on feedback from relevant stakehold-
ers. The screening tool also includes an option to calculate the absolute sustainability of projects
based on economic and grandfathering allocation methods. The sustainability ratios ranged from
0.3–1.4 in the two cases tested, with most being below the threshold of the safe operating space.
While the absolute sustainability concept is new and needs further explanation in the tool, it intro-
duces threshold thinking to impact assessments to supplement relative comparisons. The screening
tool is not a comprehensive LCA but it can provide a quick and easy way of comparing solutions
early in the planning of several types of construction projects within the utility sector1.
1ChatGPT assisted in the formulation of the abstract (OpenAI, 2025) (See full prompt and answer in Appendix
11.6.1).
==PAGE:3==
Sammenfatning
Kommuner og forsyningsselskaber st˚ar over for udfordringen med at etablere bæredygtig infrastruk-
tur for at kunne tilpasse samfundet til flere ekstreme vejrhændelser som følge af klimaforandringer.
Anlægsarbejde kræver dog b˚ade ressourcer og energi.
Livscyklusvurdering (LCA) kvantificerer
miljøp˚avirkninger, men er typisk kompleks, tidskrævende og anvendes ofte for sent i projekter til
at kunne bruges til reelle forbedringer. Form˚alet med denne rapport er derfor at undersøge, hvor-
dan en LCA kan udføres tidligt i projektperioden, og hvordan disse resultater kan kommunikeres
til beslutningstagere, s˚a de kan sammenligne alternative løsninger og identificere optimeringsmu-
ligheder i anlægsprojekter inden for forsyningssektoren.
Et screeningsværktøj blev udviklet baseret p˚a InfraLCA, et LCA-værktøj fra Vejdirektoratet. Screen-
ingsværktøjet forenkler brugerinput ved at integrere antagelser og beregninger. Tre cases blev brugt
til at identificere de datainputs, der bidrager væsentligt til det endelige resultat, herunder mængde
og materialetype for store komponenter som rør, brønde og materialer til vejkassen.
Mængder
af belægning og grus til vejkassen er baseret p˚a trafikklasser, men værdierne i screeningsværktøjet
stemmer ikke altid overens med de faktiske mængder. Transportafstande for komponenter som min-
dre rør, brønde og jordh˚andtering p˚a stedet havde begrænset betydning og kan derfor prædefineres.
Miljøp˚avirkninger fra rydning af vegetation og vejreparationer blev vurderet som ubetydelige i
screeningsøjemed for de undersøgte cases. Screeningsværktøjet antager mængden af jord og grus,
der bruges i projektet, men disse blev generelt overestimeret og bør derfor finjusteres. Den største
p˚avirkning fra b˚ade jord og grus stammer fra transporten, som ogs˚a afhænger af mængden. En
mulig løsning kunne være at give brugeren mulighed for at vælge en specifik afstand eller indføre
prædefinerede optimeringsmuligheder med reduceret transport eller mindre jord og grus i projektet.
For at gøre værktøjet brugervenligt for ikke-LCA-eksperter og sikre klarhed og ensartethed blandt
brugere blev mængden af input reduceret, og forklaringer blev tilføjet p˚a baggrund af feedback
fra relevante stakeholders. Screeningsværktøjet inkluderer ogs˚a en mulighed for at beregne pro-
jekters absolutte bæredygtighed baseret p˚a økonomiske og ”grandfathering”-fordelingsmetoder.
Bæredygtigheds-ratioen varierede fra 0,3–1,4 i de to testede cases, hvor de fleste l˚a under grænsevær-
dien for den planetære grænse for klimaforandringer. Selvom konceptet om absolut bæredygtighed
er nyt og kræver yderligere forklaring i værktøjet, introducerer det en tærskel til LCA-beregninger
som supplement til relative sammenligninger mellem løsninger. Screeningsværktøjet er ikke en fuld-
stændig LCA, men det kan give en hurtig og enkel m˚ade at sammenligne løsninger p˚a i de tidlige
faser af planlægningen af flere typer anlægsprojekter inden for forsyningssektoren2.
2ChatGPT assisted in the translation of the abstract (OpenAI, 2025) (See full prompt and answer in Appendix
11.6.1).

Preface and Acknowledgements
This master thesis is written by Katrine Rygaard Pedersen (s194456) and Emma Østergaard Vil-
lumsen (s194425) at the Department of Environmental and Resource Engineering at the Technical
University of Denmark (DTU) in collaboration with WSP Denmark. The project has been carried
out in the period from August 26th 2024 to January 26th 2025, and equals 30 ECTS for each author,
who each contributed with an equal amount of work and both vouch for the entire report. The data
used in the project has been kindly provided by WSP Denmark and KLAR Forsyning.
First and foremost, we would like to thank our primary supervisor Stig Irving Olsen (DTU Sustain)
for meeting with us often and providing important suggestions and feedback throughout the project.
Many thanks to our external supervisor Søren Gabriel (WSP Denmark) for his multiple ideas and
dedication to the project.
Thank you to the group of people who helped make this project come together.
Emilie Berg
Hansen (WSP Denmark) for providing data, helping with InfraLCA and providing feedback. Bettina
Precht Simonsen (KLAR Forsyning) for providing data about the Køge Nord project, and giving
valuable feedback on our analysis and final screening tool. Mads Lenschau and Jon Kjær Jørgensen
(Vejdirektoratet) for giving feedback on the screening tool and providing extra knowledge about
InfraLCA. Jesper Madsen (WSP Denmark) for a detailed review of our drainage section of the
screening tool - a topic otherwise very new to us. Sigurd Hoffmann Buhl (Københavns Kommune)
for the inspiration for the screening tool and the valuable insights into the communication of LCA.
Thank you to Dennis Johansen (Tscherning A/S), Julie Katrine Jensen (Norreco A/S), and Jacob
Alexander Damkjær (Vandcentersyd) for meeting with us and sharing your knowledge.
Emma Østergaard Villumsen, s194425
Katrine Rygaard Pedersen, s194456

Master Thesis
Jan 2025
1
Introduction
Going out of the year 2024, record-breaking heat and extreme weather events are more prevalent
than ever. The year 2024 was the warmest year ever since temperature recording in 1850, and
November 2023 was the warmest November globally (Copernicus, 2024). Greenhouse gas emissions
and human activities are causing a global temperature increase, and in 2019, the atmospheric con-
centrations of CO2 reached 410 ppm, the highest concentration in at least 2 million years. The risks
of extreme weather, heatwaves, droughts, and heavy precipitation are predicted to increase due to
human-induced emissions, and so are the damages (IPCC, 2023). Damages from floods in Denmark
are predicted to reach about 406 billion DKK in the next 100 years, and a significant portion of
that damage can be avoided with proper climate adaptation (Jensen, 2024b). Municipalities and
utility companies now face the challenge of providing proper infrastructure to mitigate these ex-
treme weather events.
Every time new construction is undertaken, energy and resources are consumed, inevitably leading
to emissions. With the implementation of the CSRD directive requiring increased sustainability
reporting from companies (EU, 2022), and the Danish government’s target of 70% reduction in
emissions (Energistyrelsen, 2024a), there is a growing focus on reducing environmental impacts.
One of the increasingly well-known tools for accounting environmental impacts is the method Life
Cycle Assessment (LCA). It is already well established in the building industry, with LCAByg being
used to account for emissions on multiple types of buildings (LCAByg, 2024). The Danish Road
Directorate (Vejdirektoratet) uses the tool InfraLCA to account for emissions from road and sewage
structures (Vejdirektoratet, n.d.). Tools like these aid decision makers in assessing the most optimal
solution. A challenge with LCA is that in order to accurately document emissions from a project,
a large dataset is needed - data that is often only available late in the project. When the emissions
are accounted for late in the project period, the opportunity space for alternative solutions is sig-
nificantly reduced, leaving little use for the application of the analysis to actually reduce emissions.
The purpose of this master thesis is therefore to investigate the following research question:
How can an LCA be performed early in the project period, and how can the results from such an
LCA be communicated to decision-makers to enable them to compare alternative solutions and
identify potential optimisations of construction projects within the utility sector?
The following sub questions will support the investigation:
• What are the challenges related to using LCA in decision making within the utility sector?
• Which elements, assumptions, and components are required in the calculation of projects
within the utility sector?
• How much detail and data is necessary in order to get meaningful LCA results in the early
stage of planning a project?
• Which elements are required for non-LCA experts to understand and generate comparable
results of an LCA in a screening tool across different users?
• How can the concept of absolute sustainability be implemented as a way of assessing if a
project stays within the planetary boundaries?
1
==PAGE:12==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
This will be investigated through the construction of a screening tool. The screening tool should
be able to perform a ”quick-and-dirty” LCA with little input information. Three cases will be
used as examples to analyse the opportunities and limitations of the screening tool, and relevant
stakeholders will be involved in testing and giving feedback. It is imperative that tools, that can
help reduce our environmental impacts, are accessible and offer pragmatic solutions. There is still
plenty we can do to make resilient and sustainable infrastructure. Let us therefore dive into the
world of carbon emissions, construction, and climate adaptation, and explore the carbon cost of
cobblestones, clinkers, and concrete.
2
==PAGE:13==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
2
Background
Before constructing a screening tool, current practices for quantifying environmental impacts within
construction projects are investigated. This includes a brief overview of the decision structures
when planning a construction project, interviews with relevant stakeholders, and an explanation
of existing (and emerging) tools within this field. But firstly, an introductory background will be
presented of the LCA-method, which will be expanded further in Section 3.1, and its use in regard
to decision-making.
2.1
Life Cycle Assessment
The method of Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) can be defined by four characteristics: It takes a
life cycle perspective, it covers a broad range of environmental issues, it is quantitative, and it is
based on science (Hauschild et al., 2018). The 14044 ISO-standard defines LCA as a: ”Compi-
lation and evaluation of the inputs, outputs and the potential environmental impact of a product
system throughout its life cycle” (ISO 14044:2006, 2008). The life cycle of a product or system
can be divided into different stages, and the terms used for these in this report are visualised in
Figure 1 (Masson, 2023). This designation follows the European standards of sustainability cal-
culations within construction and facilities (EN 15978) and Environmental Product Declarations
(EPDs) (EN 15804) (Dansk standard, 2012, 2019). The cyclical view is applied to prevent burden
shifting, meaning that mitigation of impacts in one life cycle stage might result in higher impacts
in another stage (Hauschild et al., 2018). LCA also covers several environmental impacts, though
climate change is often the one in focus. These include, but are not limited to, freshwater use,
toxic impacts on human health, and depletion of non-renewable resources. The reason for including
several environmental impacts is, as with the life cycle stages, to prevent burden shifting from one
impact to another (Hauschild et al., 2018).
An LCA is made up of several impacts aggregated over time and space, which requires simplifications
and generalisations in the modelling. This means that the resulting impacts are not the actual
impact, but an impact potential, and therefore LCA does not say anything specific about the ”true”
sustainability of the product, but is useful when comparing sustainability among similar products
(Hauschild et al., 2018). Many resources and details are needed to include all the processes and
stages of the life cycle for an LCA to be representative. However, it is estimated that about 80%
of all environmental effects associated with a product can already be determined in the design
phase (Hetherington et al., 2014). This motivates the development of ways to assess environmental
impacts even if data sets are scarce or coarse.
3
==PAGE:14==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Figure 1: Life cycle stages as defined by the EN 15978 and EN 15804 standards (Masson, 2023).
2.1.1
LCA in Decision-making
A review found that the use of life cycle approaches has increased in policy making in the EU. As an
example, the Ecodesign Directive introduced mandatory requirements for new products following a
life cycle approach, and the establishment of the Product- and Organisation- Environmental Foot-
print (PEF and OEF) provided LCA-methods that quantifies environmental impacts of products
and organisations (Sala et al., 2021). The concept of a life cycle is also at the centre of policies
and communications such as the European Green Deal, the Circular Economy Action Plan (Sala
et al., 2021) and the EU Taxonomy (Subal et al., 2024). Within the industry, LCA is used for
five main purposes: decision support in product development, marketing purposes, monitoring of
environmental performance of products, selection of suppliers, and strategic planning (Hauschild
et al., 2018).
A study investigating the use of LCA in decision-making found that almost half of the organisa-
tions surveyed use LCA frequently, with the other half using it in only few decision situations (Subal
et al., 2024). In companies, it was found that LCA is used for product development, optimisation,
4
==PAGE:15==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
and strategic decisions. Public authorities in Switzerland consider costs, environmental impacts
and LCA results when developing new regulations. The main factors that limit the influence of
LCA is a lower priority of environmental aspects compared to other decision criteria, complex LCA
methods and results, and lack of data to perform a reliable LCA. Other aspects also include lack
of personnel, difficulties in communicating LCA results, lack of integration of LCA as a tool in
the organisation, and high costs. Suggestions for improvement included better harmonisation and
standardisation of LCA methodology, enforcing of environmental reporting by law, interpretation
support for the results, education of personnel, awareness raising of employees and management,
and accessibility of robust data (Subal et al., 2024).
A key finding of the study was that when LCA is formally integrated and conducted in the decision-
process, the results are also considered to higher degree than if LCA is not formally integrated. That
was at least the correlation found in the questionnaire statements. During interviews with stake-
holders, the consideration of LCA results and their use in decision-making was less obvious. Criteria
such as costs, customer requirements, and simplicity of production were viewed as main drivers.
Another interviewee stated that environmental aspects were important to the company due to in-
vestors, customers, and legal requirements. Even so, some companies responded that learnings from
LCA results from previous projects are used for decisions of future projects. Others said that LCA
tools are used to educate employees, as they were put to calculate and compare environmental
impacts of different products. This allowed them to apply the learnings in their design thinking
process (Subal et al., 2024).
It was also investigated which environmental impacts are mostly assessed in companies. Here it
was found that climate change is the most used, aligning with the criteria of simplicity and data
availability (Subal et al., 2024).
2.1.2
Environmental Product Declaration (EPD)
There are different standards used for LCA-based labels and declarations. The International Stan-
dards Organisation (ISO) uses three typologies, I, II, and III, for environmental labels, with pref-
erential principles and procedures for each one of them (ISO 14021, ISO 14024, and ISO 14025).
An Environmental Product Declaration (EPD) is a type III environmental product declaration
(ISO 14025), is LCA-based and used to communicate the environmental performance of a product
(Del Borghi, 2013). ISO 14025 requires the programme operator to publish programme instructions,
product category rules (PCRs), and to register the EPD. EPDs are also third-party verified (EPD
International AB, n.d.). PCRs ensure comparability in results between different producers of the
same product. They are a collection of requirements for different product groups, that lays out how
to model the LCA, what to include, what data to use, and which environmental indicators to report
(Del Borghi, 2013). Figure 2 gives an overview of the rules and standards governing EPDs, with
PCRs being the most specific rules (Ecochain, 2024).
Two different types of EPD’s exist: an industry- and a product-specific EPD. The industry-specific
EPD represents an average of similar products from the same industry. A product-specific EPD is
created based on data on one product (EPD Danmark, 2024). The amount of EPDs are increasing,
with approximately 17,000 EPDs published globally in 2023. Especially within the construction
sector EPDs are becoming more influential due to the environmental impacts from the building
sector.
Almost half of the world’s raw materials extracted are used in the built environment,
construction generates about a third of all global waste, and it accounts for 40% of global carbon
5
==PAGE:16==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
emissions. Regulations are also driving the EPD generation forth, with the Construction Products
Regulation (CPR) in Europe and the Inflation Reduction Act (IRA) in the US that both aim at
reducing environmental impacts in construction (Ecochain, 2024).
Figure 2: LCA standards and rules that govern EPDs, sorted from the least to most specific (top
to bottom) (Ecochain, 2024).
Since 2023 it has been required by Danish law in the Building regulations to perform LCA calcula-
tions for all new buildings. Buildings above 1000 m2 are required to limit CO2-emission to 12 kg
CO2-eq per m2 per year, which, according to plan, will be lowered every second year (Social- og
boligstyrelsen, 2023). EPDs are freely available, and can be used as data input when calculating
LCAs on buildings (EPD Danmark, n.d.). This law therefore means that there is an incentive for
product manufactures to create EPDs, in order fulfil the needs of their customers. It also allows
manufacturers to compete with other companies, not only on the price, but also on the emissions
from the products. EPDs are therefore a good example of standardising and harmonising LCA
methods in order to apply it more broadly in policy and decision-making. In this report, EPDs will
be the main source of data for processes used in the LCA calculations.
2.1.3
Absolute Sustainability
The classical LCA works very well in relative terms for comparison of environmental impacts of
similar products or to identify hot-spots. But how do we assess if an option is sustainable enough?
An emerging scientific field within the world of LCA is Absolute Environmental Sustainability As-
sessment, denoted Absolute Sustainability in this report.
The definition of sustainable development, coined by the United Nations’ Brundtland Commission,
is ”development that meets the needs of the present without compromising the ability of future
generations to meet their own needs”. This statement gives a target, but the definition is vague.
Firstly, it does not specify which needs that are in question. This allows for large differences in the
resource use and environmental impacts depending on the need to be fulfilled. Secondly, the way in
which future generations will meet their needs, is also flexible in regards to the type of needs and
the way in which they will be fulfilled (Hauschild, 2015).
6
==PAGE:17==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
The Planetary Boundary concept was developed by Rockstr¨om and his colleagues (Rockstr¨om et al.,
2009). The ability of anthropogenic activities to significantly change the Earth’s ecosystems causes
human kind to leave the Holocene epoch that has been characterised by very stable temperatures
and climatic conditions, and has been the environmental foundation of human civilisation. Nine
planetary systems were identified, each with a boundary. The boundaries are based on the notion
of ensuring the stable environmental conditions known throughout the Holocene epoch (Hauschild,
2015). The planetary boundary gives a spectre of risk and uncertainty, meaning that transgressing
a boundary will not necessarily give a instant unwanted outcome, but the risk of that unwanted
outcome is significantly higher than being below that boundary (Steffen et al., 2015). An analysis
of the many interactions among the boundaries suggests that two of them, climate change and bio-
sphere integrity, are connected to all of the other planetary boundaries. Historically, different time
periods in Earth history have often been delineated by significant shifts in climate, the biosphere,
or both. This suggests that climate change and biosphere integrity should be recognised as core
planetary boundaries through which the other boundaries operate (Steffen et al., 2015). The limits
set out by the boundaries gives humanity a ’safe operating space’ in which it is safe to act within
(Hauschild, 2015).
So, we have a ”safe operating space” (SOS) to work within globally. But the framework of planetary
boundaries does not specify how we divide and allocate it between nations, companies, individuals,
and the products we use. The choice of allocation methods is an informal procedure based on sub-
jective preferences, and science can only elucidate the consequences of different principles (Andersen
et al., 2024). There is therefore no objectively correct way to do this, but several approaches have
been proposed.
One of these allocation methods is denoted Equal-per-capita and translates very well from its title
to its function: It allocates the share of the SOS equally among all humans. It uses the principle
of equality, in the meaning of equal worth of all humans. But equality can also be interpreted as
actors in equal positions and carrying equal obligations. In this understanding, equality would mean
the distribution of a greater share to the disadvantaged, reflecting different needs, responsibility,
and capacity of humans. These considerations are not taken into account in the equal-per-capita
allocation used here, but other allocation principles try to incorporate this either through e.g. an
assessment of economic capacity or a ’debt’ of previous emittances (Tilsted and Bjørn, 2023).
Another commonly used sharing principle is Grandfathering. Grandfathering dictates that future
emissions should be proportional to past emissions levels. E.g. if Denmark accounted for 5% of
the global emissions in 2000, according to grandfathering, Denmark is allowed to keep emitting
5% globally in 2025 as well. The grandfathering allocation method therefore favours high-emitters,
allowing them to keep their position as high-emitters in the future. It also requires setting a base
year, from when the portion of emissions should be accounted. This choice of base year can be
rather arbitrary. Grandfathering goes against ethical imperatives related to climate justice, but it
is still widely used due to ease of data input (Tilsted and Bjørn, 2023).
The need for combining methods appear to increase inversely with the scale of the object of study
(Ryberg et al., 2020). In this report, it is necessary to downscale to a project level. Ryberg et
al. (2020), recommend that when downscaling the SOS to industrial units, like products, or in
this case projects, a two step approach should be used. First, they recommend that for countries,
regions, and at individual scale, the SOS should be divided as a resource using the equal-per-capita
7
==PAGE:18==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
principle, due to the complexity of defining e.g. welfare or capabilities. For instance, emissions of
CO2 can be measured directly, but the welfare experienced as a result of emitting a specific amount
of CO2 is not measured easily. Secondly, the share to the industrial unit should be based on the
utility that is provided by the unit to its user. The SOS assigned to the industrial unit will there-
fore depend on the number of users and the utility the unit provides to its users (Ryberg et al., 2020).
It should be noted that the SOS likely will change over time due to new scientific realisations,
thereby changing the benchmark used in an absolute sustainability analysis (Andersen et al., 2024).
This is already seen with the updated SOS values from Petersen et al. 2022. Nevertheless, the SOS
will be used as the threshold for assessing absolute sustainability in this report, accepting these
uncertainties.
2.2
Overview of project phases
Depending on when the LCA is performed during the project time line, different levels of data are
available, and the implementation of the results varies. Projects within engineering are split into
different phases in a phase-model. Two different definitions helps illustrate the relevant project
phases in a consulting project. The first definition, seen in Figure 3 stems from the ABR (DK:
Almindelige Betingelser for R˚adgivning), the General conditions for consultancy services for building
and construction works, which is the general agreement between consultants in the construction
sector and their customers (Danske Arkitektvirksomheder, 2019). The phases are elaborated by
FRI (DK: Foreningen af R˚adgivende Ingeniører), the Danish Association of Consulting Engineers
(FRI, 2018).
Figure 3: The five project phases defined in ABR 18 § 11 (General conditions for consultancy
services for building and construction works) (ABR, 2018).
Each phase is a step through the design and implementation of the project, which are described
below:
• Programming phase: The programming phase, also known as the Idea-Phase, is where
the first processing of the customers’ thoughts, needs, and expectations about the project
takes place. In this phase ideas, visions, and goals are defined, and planners start mapping
and considering different solutions. The foundation for the solutions are made in regard to
economy, sustainability, scale, quality, and time schedule.
• Proposal phase: The consultants present their proposals for the project and receives feed-
back from the customer. FRI divides this phase into three: disposition proposal (first proposals
with more than one solution), project proposal (final solution), and finally authority proposal
(defined solution in order to apply for permits) (FRI, 2018).
• Projecting phase: After deciding on the direction of the project, the projecting starts.
The proposal and projecting phase therefore overlaps. In this phase, the detailed project is
8
==PAGE:19==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
designed, dimensioned, and planned based on the proposals, data, experience, and knowledge
about risks and possibilities. The final product is the procurement-project, which is a well
defined project, that is put into tendering to find entrepreneurs (FRI, 2018).
• Execution phase After tendering, the best offer from entrepreneurs is chosen, and the project
is then executed.
• Use phase: After completion, the project is in operation.
In between each phase, the consulting company will have to deliver an updated time schedule for
the projecting and execution of the project (ABR, 2018). When considering a project management
approach, there are so called ”gates” in between each project phase. The gates function as decision
points, where something often needs to be delivered to the customer (Geraldi et al., 2022). At each
project phase, the level of detail and amount of knowledge increase. As the project progresses, more
decisions are made, making it increasingly difficult to implement significant changes. Therefore, it
is crucial to consider sustainability as early as possible to facilitate impactful changes that reduce
the project’s environmental impact (Brudler, 2019).
2.3
Sustainability of construction projects in practice
Several stakeholders are involved in the successful execution and implementation of a climate adap-
tation projects within the utility sector. The overall responsibility for regulating climate adapta-
tion projects in Denmark falls to the state through law and regulations, as well as coordination of
combined efforts across different authorities. The municipalities are responsible for mapping out
vulnerable areas, planning and prioritising efforts, and giving permissions for projects. Utility com-
panies are then responsible for the implementation, construction, and operation of these projects
(Miljøstyrelsen, n.d.). This means that the journey from an idea to a finished construction project
often starts in the municipality but is developed and implemented in collaboration with the utility
companies (Simonsen, 2025).
Therefore, several interviews have been conducted in this report in order to get an overview of how
the utility sector, municipality, and other relevant stakeholders in Denmark work with sustainability
and their use of LCA in the decision-making process. The interviews were conducted in a semi-
structured form with open questions in order to allow for open dialogue and sharing of knowledge
(Aarhus Universitet, n.d.).
2.3.1
Municipality of Copenhagen
On the 12th of September 2024 we had a meeting with Sigurd Hoffmann and Mathias Werther
(Hoffman and Werther, 2024). Sigurd and Mathias are sustainability consultants in the Municipal-
ity of Copenhagen in the department of Mobility, climate adaptation and city maintenance (Danish:
Mobilitet, Klimasikring og Byvedligehold (MKB)). They showed how they are using the LCA tool
InfraLCA, further explained in Section 2.4.4, and integrating it with their projects. They have
modified it to be able to use it as a screening tool for construction projects in Copenhagen. They
now operate with two different tools: one which is used for political purposes, and is an impor-
tant communication tool, and one which is used for project consulting. The a risk of ”promising”
or calculating something in an LCA that is not possible in practice led to them implementing a
rather large span of uncertainties of the environmental impacts. In general, Sigurd and the team
has found that political wishes do not always align with the most beneficial solution in terms of
sustainability. Politicians have to weigh many criteria when choosing between different solutions,
9
==PAGE:20==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
and the ”greener”-solution is not necessarily the first priority (Hoffman and Werther, 2024).
Sigurd argues that in the early stage, it is not minor changes such as choice of material or less
digging, that is in question, but rather what is going to be build. The tool used in the earliest
stage in the project period does not include optimization options. Once a Bill of Materials (BOM)
of the project can be made, Sigurd makes a new and more detailed LCA using another tool, which
makes it possible to add improvements and optimizations. Often, the more sustainable solution is
economical beneficial, which is a good selling point to the politicians and project leaders (Hoffman
and Werther, 2024).
When using their tool, it is mainly CO2-eq emissions and resource use, that they focus on, as these
are easier to communicate and interpret. To make the results more relatable, midpoint categories
are normalized to person equivalents. Another way of communicating the results is done through
a speedometer. The speedometer gives an indication of how ’well’ the project performs in terms of
sustainability. Sigurd explained that the values behind the speedometer are rather arbitrary, but
that it still works well for communication purposes (Hoffman and Werther, 2024).
They chose to only work with stage A1-A5, since the C and D stages are assumed included as
A1-A5 in future projects in the same area (see Figure 1 for an overview of life cycle stages). In
Copenhagen, new constructions are never done on bare terrain, since everything is already covered
in buildings and infrastructure. They therefore work with a ”cultural life span”, since most elements
in the city could have a longer life span, but are often replaced due to political wishes or needs of
the citizens. The actual life span of the material is therefore often much higher than the cultural
life span (Hoffman and Werther, 2024).
Based on the interview with the municipality of Copenhagen, we received a lot of inspiration, ideas,
and advice for creating our own screening tool. We got an insight into how the municipality works
with planning projects and including LCA in the different gates in the planning process.
2.3.2
Vejdirektoratet
Vejdirektoratet, The Danish Road Directorate plan, construct and maintain the Danish national
roads, improves the trunk network and prepares analyses, preliminary investigations and reports
about future projects for roads and bridges (Vejdirektoratet, 2024). The Danish Road Directorate,
works with LCA in several parts of their planning process. The large road construction projects
are politically driven and need to be decided in parliament. It is often a long process over sev-
eral years (Lenschau and Jørgensen, 2024). The most important decision tool in relation to the
environmental impact of a project, is the Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA) (in Danish:
Miljøkonsekvensvurdering (MKV)) which is created in the early stages of the project period. Along
with the EIA, other criteria such as the economy, options for the placement, and the design of the
road, are also considered. In order to give the politicians the best possible suggestion for the road
design, LCA is used as a decision tool at different gates in the planning phase. LCAs are created
both in the early planning phase when only the approximate line-placement of the road is known,
and later in the detailed design of the road. After construction of the road, a final LCA is made
(Lenschau and Jørgensen, 2024).
All LCAs are created in the LCA tool InfraLCA, further described in Section 2.4.4. In InfraLCA
it is possible to import the Bill of Materials (BOM) and calculate the LCA based on the materials
10
==PAGE:21==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
from there. The road design phase has many iterations, and every time something is changed or
optimized, a new BOM is created. For every new iteration, a new LCA based on the new BOM
is created and the result is added to a ”CO2-log”, where the goal is to continuously decrease the
total CO2-eq emission from the project (Lenschau and Jørgensen, 2024). The possibilities to change
things in the project significantly decrease after tendering, where the BOM is settled, and where it
is the entrepreneurs that make the final decisions.
The road design process is a constant negotiation between different professions. Finding the bal-
ance between climate footprint and road construction can be difficult. Often the results show that
projects with lower CO2-eq emissions are also more cost effective, which automatically helps push
the ”greener” alternative, although it also requires more consulting hours in order to calculate the
LCAs. The sector is currently pressured by inflated prices which makes economy an even more
important decision variable (Lenschau and Jørgensen, 2024).
InfraLCA calculates all emissions of the materials used in the projects, but the Danish Road Direc-
torate has also developed a separate program that can filter the resulting emissions to only include
national emissions. This feature is added in order to be able to consider the Danish Climate Law
which includes a 70 % reduction target of national emissions in 2030 (Energistyrelsen, 2024b). As
most of their projects are to be implemented far out in the future, they also consider the future Dan-
ish projections of energy sources and material use in their calculation of future emissions (Lenschau
and Jørgensen, 2024).
The main takeaways from the meeting with the Danish Road Directorate, was their insights into
the usage of LCA on projects, and their development of their own tool InfraLCA. It shows, that
if the right tool is available, companies are also more willing to use it. It also pointed out the
importance of having decision-makers prioritise LCA as a tool for decision, since this means that
the LCA calculations will actually be made and used. Prices are expected to increase, and there
is therefore a risk that economy will have a higher weight in final decision. Even so, reductions in
emissions seem to often go hand in hand with reductions in cost.
2.3.3
KLAR Forsyning
On Monday 6th of January 2025, a meeting was held with project leader Bettina Precht Simonsen
at the utility company KLAR Forsyning in Køge (Simonsen, 2025). KLAR Forsyning has a sustain-
ability strategy with a focus on reducing emissions from their projects and procurements. Bettina
told that the challenge is how to transfer a strategy into action. Several meetings have been held
internally with a focus on sharing experience and solutions that help increase sustainability aware-
ness. Bettina told how this has helped creating a bigger awareness of implementing sustainability
thinking into the organisation. However, prioritising sustainable solutions is still a challenge, e.g.
new products with lower carbon emissions might be more expensive and it is often easier to use
what is already known (Simonsen, 2025).
LCA is not used much in KLAR Forsyning yet. It is complicated, time consuming and requires a lot
of data, and not many in the organisation are educated within the use of LCA. For now, it is only
the large projects, such as wastewater treatment plants, where resources can be allocated to perform
an LCA. It would be too expensive to perform it on smaller projects, such as pipe projects. Here, it
might be beneficial to make a standard calculation that can apply to several types of pipe projects,
including sewage, drinking water, and district heating. Bettina emphasises that it is important to
11
==PAGE:22==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
understand the technical and practical parts of the installation and construction in the projects in
order to make a precise LCA. There is concern that the results will be too imprecise and uncertain
to be used. But she says that it is important to know that an LCA does not give a complete pic-
ture of reality, but rather an approximation that can be used to compare solutions (Simonsen, 2025).
When planning a project there are several considerations such as cost, operational reliability, and
nutrient discharge. Sustainability is therefore just one out of several parameters to be considered.
In KLAR Forsyning they use a multi-criteria analysis for their sustainability analysis based on ob-
jectives from the EU Taxonomy (Simonsen, 2025). For each objective they qualitatively assess the
impact of their project by using a traffic light categorisation with green, yellow, and red markings.
This gives an overview of the environmental impacts in the early stages of planning. Bettina said
that a screening tool for screening environmental impacts would be useful at the disposition pro-
posal, where several solutions are presented and considered, and could be a supplement to their
multi-criteria model (Simonsen, 2025).
The key takeaways from the interview with Bettina are the need for being able to communicate and
get people onboard with the sustainability agenda. A screening tool could be a good supplement
to quantify sustainability in the early project phase, where several ideas are being weighted and
compared. The screening tool should be cheap, intuitive, and easy to use for non-LCA experts, and
it should be clear how the results are used and interpreted.
2.3.4
VandCenter Syd
VandCenter Syd is a Danish utility company in Odense and works with distribution of drinking
water and treatment and drainage of sewage and rainwater (VandCenter Syd, n.d.). On Thursday
the 9th of January 2025 we conducted an interview with Jacob Alexander Damkjær, project leader
in their department for sustainable development, but also project leader on the new LCA tool
ForsyningsLCA, currently in development and further described in Section 2.4.3 (Damkjær, 2025).
In order to quantify the environmental impacts from projects, VandCenter Syd has been using a
screening tool called StormLCA, made by Sarah Brudler Friis during her PhD. This tool is not
publicly available. It can calculate environmental impacts from rainwater pipes and also assess
the nutrient discharge throughout its lifetime. Jacob mainly uses it in the early planning phase
where different solutions are proposed. Here, he compares the solutions both in terms of economy,
carbon emissions, nutrient discharge, use of gravel, and biodiversity. The comparisons are sent to
the planning team, where the different parameters are weighted and a solutions is chosen. Jacob
mentions that the weighting of parameters is not factually based, and in terms of weighing sustain-
ability, there has not been enough competencies to weigh it properly. StormLCA has also been an
inspiration for creating ForsyningsLCA (Damkjær, 2025).
When Jacob started his job at VandCenter Syd, StormLCA had not been used in a long time. This
was due to lack of employees with LCA experience and knowledge, and Jacob has therefore started
using it again. Being the only person using this tool, he found it challenging to distribute knowledge
on sustainability to the whole organisation. Therefore, he focused on implementing sustainability
in the strategy. With sustainability being implemented in the strategy, Jacob has experienced how
project leaders now address him for advice on environmental impacts of different projects (Damkjær,
2025).
12
==PAGE:23==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
VandCenter Syd occasionally uses consulting firms to assess the sustainability of their projects.
Jacob says that this of course gives a result, but it is not always transparent how the result is
calculated. Therefore, Jacob sees the value in making it possible for utility companies to be able to
perform their own LCAs, which is also the purpose of ForsyningsLCA (Damkjær, 2025).
In 2019, a report on the absolute sustainability, a term described in Section 2.1.3, of VandCenter Syd
was conducted in order to investigate if all the activities related to the water supply and wastew-
ater treatment in VandCenter Syd can be considered absolute sustainable within the framework
of planetary boundaries. Potential activities to reduce environmental impacts were also identified.
It was found that the largest exceedance of the safe operating space was in the impact category
Biochemical flow of emissions of phosphorus (P) and nitrogen (N), and Climate change impacts.
The main source of emissions for P and N was from wastewater treatment. The main contributors
for climate change were electricity use, methane emissions from the sewage network, and carbon
emissions from cement production. The conclusion from the report is therefore that these are some
of the areas to prioritise in order to limit environmental impacts (Ryberg et al., 2019).
Another finding of the report was the contribution of the overall impacts in VandCenter Syd from
operation and construction activities. On average, operation and construction accounted for about
60% and 40% respectively across the impact categories. The main contribution to construction is
concrete production, while the contribution to operational activities is a mix of electricity and emis-
sions from the storm- and wastewater network, and production of iron sulfate for the wastewater
treatment process. For the biochemical flows, it is mainly operational impacts that make up the
environmental impact, in particular due to P and N in wastewater effluent (Ryberg et al., 2019).
There could therefore be an incentive to integrate the use phase when accounting for environmen-
tal impacts in an LCA screening tool, as a significant amount of the contribution also lies in the
operation of utility works.
Furthermore, the interview with Jacob showed the importance of having competent employees that
have knowledge on LCA in order to be able to implement LCA thinking in an organisation. It is
also important to anchor sustainable thinking in the vision and goals of the organisation in order for
it to become a priority. Transparency in the calculation of LCAs aid the confidence of the results,
and it is therefore important that assumptions in LCA tools are clear and understandable for the
user.
2.4
Existing LCA Tools
As more attention has been brought to sustainability throughout the years, several tools already
exists to assist decision-makers in regard to accounting for environmental impacts within the and
construction and infrastructure projects. A knowledge synthesis of those, we have found relevant
to the workings in this report are presented in the following sections.
2.4.1
LCAByg
LCAByg is a Danish LCA tool for assessing the impacts from buildings. The tool is developed
by Build, a research group at The University of Aalborg (AAU) (LCAByg, 2024).
It is open
access, and used by many stakeholders in the building sector, including architects, entrepreneurs,
and consultants, since it can be used in different phases of the process: both planning, design,
projecting, and documentation (Kanafani et al., 2021). As mentioned in Section 2.1.2, the LCA
13
==PAGE:24==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
documentation is important, due to the requirements from the Danish Building Legislation of LCA
calculations on all new buildings (Bygningsreglementet, 2024). LCAByg includes all the stages (A-
D), including the use phase (B), although the D stage, future reuse, recycling or energy recovery,
is not included in the final result that is held up against the requirements (Jørgensen et al., 2020).
LCAByg harmonizes LCA methodology across the entire building sector by providing a single tool
that can be used by all relevant stakeholders. This ensures less variability in the way results are
calculated and makes comparisons more reliable.
2.4.2
LCAWater
LCAWater is a newly developed tool (2024), created in collaboration between the two consulting
companies COWI and Envidan.
The tool is able to calculate the environmental impacts from
projects related to drinking water distribution, drainage, and climate adaptation (Envidan Den-
mark, 2024). Before the launch of the tool, Sarah Brudler Friis, one of the project leaders on the
development of LCAWater, mentioned that the water-sector is in great need of a sector standard
for LCA calculations, and she argues that a common tool is necessary in order to align the method.
The tool should be able to calculate the emissions from construction projects within the sector, and
not only operational-emissions which has previously had the biggest focus (Brudler, 2022).
LCAWater includes predefined assemblies, which are examples of different elements often used within
the sector. Engineers from Envidan and COWI summarised the main functions of LCAWater, at a
DANVA conference, as such: ”It is currently possible to calculate CO2 emissions in LCAwater on
various types (assemblies) of well and pipe material, trenches in asphalt and on bare ground, concrete
trenches and open ditches as well as basins with and without treatment, including infiltration basins,
plastic cassettes and wet pools with membrane.” (Poulsen et al., 2024). The tool is currently not
public for download and use, which is why we do not know how the tool functions in practice.
2.4.3
ForsyningsLCA
ForsyningsLCA (UtilityLCA) is an LCA tool currently in development, which is planned to be
launched by the end of 2025. The partnership behind the tool consists of eight utility companies,
and the two inter-branch organizations for district heating, and drinking- and wastewater. The
partners seek to develop a tool for streamlining the method and data use within the sector, in
order to be able to compare projects across different utility companies, along with creating common
benchmarking for future projects. The hope is that the new LCA tool will inspire planners, and
helps share ideas and innovations, and push for more collaboration within the sustainable topic in
the utility sector (Christiansen, 2024). According to a DANVA survey from 2022, 85% of the Dan-
ish utility companies are actively working with the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs). They
therefore argue that most utilities in Denmark have an interest in calculating their CO2-eq emissions
to obtain more knowledge about sustainability within their own companies (Feilberg, 2023).
ForsyningsLCA has compiled a document with specifications of requirements, which gives insight
into the wishes and needs of the partnership (Christiansen et al., 2024). ForsyningsLCA defines their
overall purpose, target group, and relevant project phases for usage. The document includes a thor-
ough overview of possible projects within water, drainage, and heating supply, and which processes
are included in each project type. In this document, ForsyningsLCA, also defines all LCA-technical
elements, such as their goal, scope, methods and standards, requirements for comparison (defini-
tion of functionality and reference period), life cycle stages, system boundaries, impact categories,
14
==PAGE:25==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
and more. It is their wish to enlighten the four main impact categories: climate change, water
use, biodiversity, and environmental contaminants (Christiansen et al., 2024). The developers of
ForsyningsLCA are yet to figure out how the tool should be developed, what is should look like,
and who should be in charge of developing the tool (Damkjær, 2025).
2.4.4
InfraLCA
InfraLCA is an LCA tool developed by The Danish Road Directorate. It was developed with a
focus on planning, operating, and constructing infrastructures and was made in order to give a uni-
form way of calculating environmental impacts for projects within infrastructure (Vejdirektoratet,
n.d.). InfraLCA is Excel-based and free to use. It is based on the EN15804:2012+A2:2019 standard
for EPDs, which includes 13 environmental impact categories (Vejdirektoratet, n.d.). Even so, In-
fraLCA lacks updated data for impact categories other than ”total-CO2-eq” (GWP) as they have
changed EPD practice from standard EN15804:A1 to A2 (Vejdirektoratet, n.d.). Emission factors
are mostly based on averages of EPDs or data from other other databases, like ¨Okobau and Ecoin-
vent. InfraLCA covers most stages in the life cycle displayed in Figure 1 (Masson, 2023), including
A1-A3, A4, A5, C1-C4, and D. B4, Replacement, is the only use-stage included (Vejdirektoratet,
n.d.).
The functional unit in InfraLCA is decided by the user. Often it would be ”one project” of a certain
type that the user creates. This makes it difficult to compare different types of road- or railway
projects. The projects can be complex, which is a challenge when trying to define a functional unit
that would cover the entire range of projects calculated in InfraLCA.
In this report, we will use the latest version of InfraLCA, v. 3.10, as a basis for developing our
LCA screening tool for projects within the utility sector.
InfraLCA can be downloaded from:
www.vejdirektoratet.dk/infralca/download-og-vejledning
15
==PAGE:26==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
3
Methodology
Here, an overview of the LCA method is presented, together with the allocations methods used in
the absolute sustainability assessment.
3.1
LCA Methodology
”If there is no unambiguous way of modelling the life cycle of a product, then there is a subjectively
best way.” (Werner, 2005) In his book, Ambiguities in Decision-oriented Life Cycle Inventories,
Frank Werner reflects upon whether LCA can be conceived as an objective natural science or
as a subjective, interest related construction.
LCA has been developed within natural science
thinking, but there are also subjective elements that depend on the perspective and the context of
the modeller. LCA is a simplified ’unit-world, input-output model’, where the inventory model is
more or less bound to the available knowledge, model assumptions and the context of the model and
application. It should therefore be seen as a decision support tool, and not as a stand-alone-truth
(Werner, 2005). Even so, several standards have been developed, as touched upon in Section 2.1.2,
in order to streamline methodological choices and harmonise results. The following sections will
give an overview of the key elements in LCA methodology within ISO 14040 (Hauschild et al., 2018)
and how they are approached in this report.
3.1.1
Goal Definition
The LCA framework operates iteratively in four phases: Goal and scope definition, Inventory anal-
ysis, Impact assessment, and Interpretation (Hauschild et al., 2018). In this report, LCAs will be
made based on three different cases and will be made with different degrees of detail. The intended
application of the results is to investigate the level of detail and data needed to get a meaningful
result. An analysis of hot-spots and a sensitivity analysis of input parameters is also performed in
order to see, which processes are most impactful and sensitive in construction projects in the utility
sector. This helps guide the level of detail needed in the screening tool and which parameters are
most important to focus on.
As mentioned in Section 2.4.4, not all emission factors have been updated in alignment with standard
EN15804:2012+A2:2019, and it is therefore only CO2-eq emissions that are assessed in this report
making it equivalent to a carbon footprint calculation. As also mentioned in Section 2.4.4, InfraLCA
does not account for the use phase, except B4 (replacement). All elements in the use phase, apart
from B4, will therefore be excluded from the scope in all calculations in this report.
3.1.2
Scope Definition
A functional unit defines the qualitative and quantitive aspects of the product or system (Hauschild
et al., 2018). Defining a functional unit for a utility project can be challenging, as there are several
aspects that can be difficult to quantify. In her PhD, Sarah Brudler Friis looks into functional
units for storm water management (SWM) systems. Some define them by the area, while others
use the volume of runoff. The primary function of SWM is to prevent flooding. A way of defining
this could be to add a specification of the rain event, the system is able to handle (Brudler, 2019).
ForsyningsLCA (presented in Section 2.4.3) propose that the functional unit for rain- and wastew-
ater projects could include economic investment in the project, hectare of reduced area, persons
serviced (PE), meters of water-transport, volume of retained water, and flow of water (Christiansen
16
==PAGE:27==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
et al., 2024). In this report, the functional unit will be defined specifically for each case.
The temporal scope of the functional unit for SWM systems vary, with some elements lasting up
a century.
Many studies therefore use a time period between 10 and 50 years (Brudler, 2019).
According to Danish building regulations, BR18, the climate impact of buildings shall be assessed
in a period of 50 years (Bygningsreglementet, 2024). ForsyningsLCA recommend a default life time
of 75 yeas for rain- and wastewater projects, which they assume is the ”operational lifetime” of
the projects (Christiansen et al., 2024). InfraLCA use a default of 50 years (Vejdirektoratet, n.d.).
50 years will therefore be used as a default life time of construction projects, if nothing else is stated.
As briefly touched upon in the interview with Jacob Alexander Damkjær from VandCenter Syd in
Section 2.3.4, the LCA tool StormLCA also accounts for nutrient discharge. A drainage system
often helps limit the amount of pollutants and nutrients reaching recipients. It would therefore be
more precise to incorporate water quality assessment, if the full function of the system is to be
included (Brudler, 2019), as biochemical flows was also proved to be a significant emission from
utility works in the absolute sustainability assessment of VandCenter Syd (Ryberg et al., 2019). In
this report, only CO2-eq emissions are assessed, and no consideration is therefore taken to the water
quality.
3.1.3
LCI and Representativeness of Data
The Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) lists all the quantities of the resources and processes used to pro-
vide the function of the system or product in question (Hauschild et al., 2018). Data used for the
processes in this report will be from EPDs, explained in Section 2.1.2, and existing processes in
InfraLCA.
The LCAs in this report only cover projects in Denmark, and it is therefore prioritised to find data
that is representative to Denmark. The geographical representativeness of data used in processes
in InfraLCA is assumed to be fairly high, as InfraLCA is developed for the Danish Road Directory
and with the Danish market in mind. In regard to time-related representativeness, new data is
prioritised. Data in InfraLCA mainly consists of averages of EPDs and data from other databases,
and it is regularly updated (Vejdirektoratet, n.d.). Therefore, the temporal representativeness is
also assumed to be high. Lastly, the data in InfraLCA is assumed to represent the products and
processes used in Danish construction projects well, and the technological representativeness is there
deemed as high. Data from other EPDs in this report is, if not otherwise stated, assumed to have
both a high geographical, temporal, and technological representativeness, as it is sought to use
EPDs of products used in Denmark, valid EPDs (they often expire within five years), and EPDs
that represent the specific product well.
Based on this, the data quality of data used in this report is based on the following self-defined
categorisation:
• 1. Data originates from an EPD that is the exact same type of product as the one used in the
case. It can also be an EPD with a product made of the same type of material and company
but with different dimensions and adjusted to the product used in the case.
• 2. Data originates from an average of EPDs that resembles the product used in the case.
• 3. Data originates from a single EPD or a single study that is similar to the product used in
the case.
17
==PAGE:28==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
• 4. Data is based on data from databases, such as Ecoinvent or ¨Okobau.
This categorisation is added to all LCIs presented in the report.
The lifetime of many of the products used in this report are expected to be 100 years in the EPDs.
That means that several uncertainties are tied to the C and D stages, as it is expected that the
world is much different 100 years from now. More focus will therefore be on the production stage
(A1-A3), transportation (A4), and construction installation processes and transportation (A5), but
C and D will still be reported and discussed.
3.1.4
Sensitivity and Uncertainty Analysis
The sensitivity of a model describes to what extent that variation of an input parameter or other
choices in the model affects the end result (Hauschild et al., 2018). In other words, if you change one
input, e.g. the amount of concrete used in a pipe, and it significantly changes the overall emissions
of the project, then the concrete input is a sensitive parameter in the model. In some literature,
this is referred to as a local sensitivity analysis (Hauschild et al., 2018). Here, this method will only
be referred to as a sensitivity analysis. In this report, a sensitivity analysis will be performed using
the method of the relative sensitivity coefficient S:
S = ∆output/output
∆input/input
(1)
, where ∆output is the change in the output as the result in the change in the input, ∆input,
while output is the baseline result and input is the original input for the baseline result (Hauschild
et al., 2018). As done in the Master Thesis by Hansen (2023), a parameter/input is considered to
have a large sensitivity when |S| ≥0.5 and a medium sensitivity when |S| ≥0.3. In this report,
a categorisation of a minor sensitivity when |S| ≥0.05 is added, while anything below is deemed
negligible. This limit is rather arbitrary, but chosen in order to elucidate changes in smaller input
categories, and not overlook other potential optimisations by being blinded by the ’big sinners’.
The uncertainties associated with LCA calculations in this project will mainly be addressed quali-
tatively, since specific distributions of data inputs are mostly unavailable. Uncertainty is embedded
in all parts of the LCA calculation, e.g. in processes in InfraLCA, and in EPDs that are complete
LCA studies on their own. There is no tradition for reporting uncertainty in the resulting EPD
document, so potential uncertainties cannot be derived from these. This embedded uncertainty will
be accepted as an assumption throughout this report.
3.2
Absolute Sustainability Allocation Methods
The possibility of performing an analysis of the absolute sustainability of the projects is added to
the screening tool in order to give an indication of whether the project’s impact stays within the
planetary boundaries. The steps required for such an analysis are described here.
As recommended in Ryberg et al. (2020), the sharing of the safe operating space (SOS) between
industrial units should be done in two steps:
• First, the global SOS is distributed using the principle of equal-per-capita. Equal-per-capita
was recommended in the short-term, as the required data is easily available, and the calcula-
tions are straightforward (Ryberg et al., 2020).
18
==PAGE:29==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
• Secondly, as the global SOS has been distributed equally amongst all people, the industrial
unit, in this case a drainage project, should be assigned a share of the SOS based on its utility,
cost, and/or the number of people using it (Ryberg et al., 2020).
The first distribution of the global SOS is therefore based on an equal-per-capita, and is formalised
in Equation 2. Here, Denmark is given a share of the global SOS based on its population:
SOSDK = PopDK
PopGlo
· SOSGlo
(2)
, where SOSDK is the safe operating space allocated to Denmark, PopDK is the population in Den-
mark, PopGlo is the global population, and SOSGlo is the global safe operating space (Tilsted and
Bjørn, 2023). An average of the actual and projected population from year 2022 to 2030 will be
used as input. For the screening tool, only GHG emissions will be assessed in regard to the absolute
sustainability. Therefore, the SOS will correspond to the SOS of GHG emissions in the following
calculations.
An updated value of the SOS for GHG is found in Petersen et al. (2022) of 2.51 Gt CO2-eq/year.
This value is computed with AR6 data (data from the 6th IPCC assessment report, published
in 2021). It is a weighted average of substance-specific contributions. 20 GHGs are included in
the calculation, including CO2, CH4, and N2O. Several variables affect the modelling of the SOS,
including reduction of land area and CO2-uptake of plants into soil. The magnitude of the SOS
therefore relies on the fulfilment of crucial assumptions. The AR6 data lists values for the confidence
intervals for lifetime radiative efficiencies and the global warming potential for 5 out the 20 GHGs
in the analysis. Based on this, a 95% confidence interval was made for the SOS, resulting in the 2.51
Gt CO2-eq/year (Petersen et al., 2022), which will be used as the reference for the SOS in this report.
The second distribution of the SOS from national level to project level is less obvious. As mentioned,
it can be done according to the utility of the project (Ryberg et al., 2020), but utility can be
measured in a number of ways.
Therefore, several approaches were examined.
The first is an
economic allocation, based on the estimated cost of the project. Here, it is assumed that the share
of the available Danish GHGs should be based on the ratio, based on the investment costs of the
project divided with the total GDP in Denmark. This allocation can also be denoted as consumption
based. The newest available GDP is used, which is from 2023 in this report, and the method is
expressed in Equation 3.
%Share = Project Cost
GDPDK
(3)
The second approach is seeking to base the allocation on the amount of utility the project provides.
It is expressed in the number of people affected/benefitting from the project. The share is combined
with the total expenditure on drainage projects in Denmark and calculated as seen in Equation 4:
%Share = People affected
PopDK
· Total Costs of Drainage ProjectsDK
GDPDK
(4)
The third allocation is based on the principle of grandfathering. In her master thesis, Hansen (2023)
estimated the GHG emissions from the Danish drainage industry. The calculation is based on three
different drainage projects, from which she calculated an average carbon intensity of 2.61E-05 CO2-
eq/DKK. This carbon intensity is multiplied with the total investments made in the industry in
order to calculate the total carbon footprint of the industry, with year 2022 as baseline (Hansen,
19
==PAGE:30==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
2023). This carbon intensity for drainage projects is used in the third allocation method as a way
of estimating the emissions of the project in 2022. Equation 5 shows the principle behind the third
allocation:
%Share = NPI · Project Cost2022 · 2.61 · 10−5CO2/DKK
Total emissionsDK, 2022
(5)
The total emissions in Denmark in 2022 are estimated to be 64.14 million tonnes CO2-eq, and
are based on the consumption based emissions (Energistyrelsen, 2024a), since the calculations in
Hansen (2023) and in this report include all scope 1, 2, and 3 emissions. The user gives an estimate
of the present investment cost of the project, e.g. for a project in 2025. As the carbon intensity
from Hansen (2023) is representative of 2022, the project cost should ideally also be representing the
equivalent cost of the same project made in 2022. This would require an accounting of the inflation
rate for the cost of future projects or a constant update on the comparative prices as of now to
2022. For now, a simple approach will be to multiply the project cost with 0.91, denoted NPI in
Equation 5. This number is found using the net price index calculator from Statistics Denmark
(Danmarks Statistik, n.d.). Here, the relative index from December 2024 to January 2022 is found,
meaning that 1 DKK in December 2024 correspond to 0.91 DKK in January 2022. This is not a
perfect solution, but it does account for some of the inflation that has happened since 2022.
Based on the allocation, a sustainability ratio (SR) can be calculated. This gives an indication of
whether the project is shooting above or below the absolute sustainability threshold, defined by the
SOS. The calculation is seen in Equation 6:
SR = Project Impact · Life Time
%Share · SOSDK
(6)
, where SR is the sustainability ratio, the %Share is the allocated share of project based on either
equation 3, 4, or 5, and SOSDK is the safe operating space allocated to Denmark based on equal
per capita. When SR is above 1, the impacts from the project exceed the allocated share of the
SOS, and when SR is below 1, the emissions of the project are within the planetary boundaries.
The project impact is divided by the life time of the project, as the SOS is given in impact per year.
In the screening tool, only the first and the third allocation method will be used, referring to
Equation 3 and 5, and an average of the SR based on these will be used. It has proven difficult
to assess the number of people that are affected by the projects. It has therefore been decided
not to include Equation 4 allocation method, as the input would be very uncertain. The resulting
calculation can be seen in the screening tool, Appendix 11.1.1, Sheet ’Absolut Bæredygtighed’.
20
==PAGE:31==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
4
The Screening Tool
The key product in this report is an LCA screening tool that can be used for different types of
projects within the utility sector. The main purpose is to create a screening tool that can be used
in the early stages of the project, requires less input data than a comprehensive LCA, is user-
friendly, and generates interpretable results accessible to non-LCA experts. This section will go
through how the tool is constructed, the assumptions and calculations used, and how the results
are generated and displayed.
The screening tool is build upon the existing Excel-based LCA tool, InfraLCA. In the developer
section of Excel, the tool Visual Basic for Application (VBA) makes it possible to create customised
functions that work ’behind’ the sheets and userforms, that the user can interact with. Userforms
are separate displays that pop up with prompting. It allows for easier use of complicated Excel-files
by simplifying which information needs to be given as input (Microsoft, 2022).
When the screening tool is opened, the first thing the user does is choose a project category. The
categories are Roads, Pipes, Ditches, Retention Basins, and the combined Drainage Project, which
will all be explained in detail in Section 4.1. In each category, the user is asked to add relevant
inputs for the LCA screening. An example of the input userform display for the category ’Pipes’
can be seen in Figure 4. After the calculation, all results will be gathered into figures and tables in
a result page in the tool. Lastly, the user can choose to create extra scenarios for comparison with
the baseline scenario and thereby try to optimize their project.
Figure 4: Screenshot of the input userform to the ”Pipes” category in the screening tool.
21
==PAGE:32==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
4.1
Formulation of Project Categories
Several categories were formulated for the use of the screening tool. Within each category, assump-
tions of amounts and combinations of materials are described in the following subsections.
4.1.1
Road
The first category is Road, denoted ”Vej” in Danish in the screening tool. This category covers
roads with different surfaces. When constructing a road, different layers are required in the road
section depending on its resistance to frost and traffic. A conceptual drawing is shown in Figure 5.
Figure 5: A conceptual drawing of layers used in road construction. The binding layer is only
relevant in asphalt road.
The bottom of the road section is the subgrade soil. Subgrade soil can have varying properties,
some with better carrying capacities, and some with low frost susceptibility. If small amounts of
the soil is very soft, it can be excavated and replaced with soil with a larger carrying capacity. If
there are larger amounts, methods such as draining can be used (Bolet and Busch, 2016). In the
screening tool, treatments of subgrade soil is not part of the scope.
The next layer in Figure 5 is the subbase course or subbase gravel (DK: Bundsikring). The purpose
of this layer is to increase the frost resistance and the carrying capacity of the road. It also functions
as a drainage pathway for potential water penetrating the road. The thickness of the subbase layer
depends on the frost susceptibility of the subgrade soil and the traffic class (Bolet and Busch, 2016).
The resistance to traffic is defined by the amount of heavy transport that can drive on the surface,
with heavy transport being defined as vehicles with a total weight above 3.5 tonnes (Vejdirektoratet,
2022). Table 1 shows the definition of traffic class and the depth of subbase gravel needed in order
to fulfil the requirements for three levels of frost susceptibility. These are the values used in in
the screening tool. The first level, frost resistant soil, is characterised by sandy soil with small
amounts of silt, while moderately frost susceptible soil consists of clay soil, also only small amounts
of silt. Highly frost susceptible soil is rich in silt or lime with pathways for water to enter (Danske
Anlægsgartnere, 2015).
22
==PAGE:33==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Table 1: An overview of the definition of traffic class (T) and the amount of subbase gravel needed
in the road section depending on the frost susceptibility and traffic class (Danske Anlægsgartnere,
2015; Vejdirektoratet, 2022).

After the subbase gravel, depending on the function and surface of the road, a support layer of
unbound and/or bound materials are used to distribute the pressure preventing tension to reach
the bottom and risk creating deformations, maintain the carrying capacity (Bolet and Busch, 2016).
Different unbound materials can be used, such as base course (DK: Stabilgrus), crushed concrete
and roof brick tiles, and macadam. Base course can be used in a large variety of circumstances, while
macadam is less useful in projects with pipe trenches due to difficulties re-establishing macadam.
Crushed concrete can technically be used the same way as base course, but if it is mixed with
bricks the quality decreases, as bricks are more easily broken down to smaller pieces than concrete
(Hejlesen, 2017). For simplicity, the unbound materials used in the calculation in the screening tool
is base course. Bound materials used in the support layer can be cement with a mix of either slag or
fly ash which creates a hydraulic binding of the materials. Hydraulic bound support layers should
only be used in places with few or no pipe trenches as they are difficult to reestablish (Hejlesen,
2017). Layers with hydraulic binding are not used in the screening tool.
When constructing an asphalt road, a binding layer is often added on top of the supporting layer
(Bolet and Busch, 2016). ATB (Asphalt Treated Base) can be used, either as a binding and/or
supporting layer (Hejlesen, 2017). In Danish, ATB is abbreviated ”GAB” (DK: Grusasfaltbeton)
and is split into different categories depending on the structure and use. ATB II is the simplest
product with a low content of binder and pebble sizes up to 32 mm. It is mainly used on highways
and other roads with heavy transport. ATB I has a higher content of binder and pebbles in sizes
up to 22 mm. It is mainly used as a lower supporting layer on roads with medium traffic. Finally,
ATB 0 has the highest amount of binder and smaller pebbles in better quality. ATB 0 is mainly
used as upper supporting layer on less trafficked roads (Karl and Christiansen, 2021). ATB 0 and I
are used in the screening tool, but only when constructing asphalt concrete road. In InfraLCA the
amount of ATB is given i m2 and the amount in the screening tool will therefore be based on the
densities used in InfraLCA. Table 2 gives an overview of the amounts used in the screening tool for
the supporting layers. ATB is used in combination with base course.

The surface layer also requires different amounts of material depending on its resistance to traffic.
Table 3 shows the amount needed to meet the requirements within each traffic class for each option
available to the user (Danske Anlægsgartnere, 2015; Vejdirektoratet, 2022). The userform in the
screening tool is programmed in a way that only makes it possible for the user to choose the surface
type that fits with the chosen traffic class. If the surface layer consists of tiles or cobblestones, screed
is often used to smooth the surface and fill in the gaps between the stones. It normally consists of
0-8 mm gravel. In the screening tool screed is denoted as ”Afretningslag” and consists of a 3 cm
deep layer of sand (Sandfyld in InfraLCA).
The user can choose whether the road is being build on an existing road or not. The existing road is
always assumed to be asphalt road. If there is an existing road, resources are spend on demolishing
the existing road. In the screening tool it is assumed that the asphalt is scraped off and disposed,
and all layers in the road section are compiled in the process ”Opbryde ubundne lag til depot” in
InfraLCA, meaning that they are all classified as unbound materials and are transported to a depot.
If there is no existing road, soil is excavated and disposed off, equivalent to the amount needed for
materials in the road section. It is always assumed that no soil or materials from the previous road
section is reused.
