4.1.2
Pipes
The second category in the screening tool is Pipes, denoted ”Ledninger” in Danish. This category
covers the integration of different types and sizes of pipes, and the user can choose either a separate
or combined sewage system (one or two pipes in the same pipe trench). When integrating pipes
into the ground, a pipe trench is excavated. The size of the pipe trench depends on the depth, the
size of the pipe and whether the pipe is integrated on existing paved or unpaved ground. The user
chooses between a range of depths: 1-2 m, 2-3 m, and 3-5 m. The average depth in each range is
used as the depth for the pipe trench, with 0.1 m extra added as buffer for a gravel support layer
beneath the pipe.
The existing ground is paved, the trench can be either braced or unbraced (DS 475:2012). In the
screening tool, it is assumed that the trench is being braced. The bracing equipment and installation
are not accounted for in the calculation. With bracing, the cross section of the pipe trench forms
a rectangle, as seen in Figure 6. The width, b, of the pipe trench is based on assumptions from
drainage engineer Jesper Madsen, WSP Denmark, and interpolations of these assumptions based
on different pipe sizes (Madsen, 2024). Detailed calculations of this can be seen in Appendix 11.2.1.
25
==PAGE:36==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Figure 6: Conceptual drawing of a pipe trench underneath paved and unpaved grounds. b is the
width of the pipe trench underneath unpaved grounds. b1 is the width of the bottom of the pipe
trench on unpaved grounds and b2 is the width of the slope of the pipe trench on unpaved grounds.
d is the depth of the pipe trenches.
When choosing paved ground, the existing pavement is assumed to be asphalt road. Asphalt and
unbound materials are removed, and once the pipe is laid, the pipe trench will be filled with soil
and/or gravel, but leave space for the road section, corresponding to the amount of asphalt and
unbound materials removed. If one wants to combine the pipe trench with a new road, the category
’Drainage Project’ has to be chosen (see Section 4.1.5). The road removed is assumed to be the
same size as the pipe trench excavated.
When choosing the unpaved ground, the pipe trench goes from forming a rectangle to forming
a trapeze, as depicted in Figure 6.
The width of the pipe trench is, as in the pipe trench on
paved grounds, based on the size and depth of the pipes. Calculations are based on estimations
and assumptions from Jesper Madsen, drainage engineer at WSP, and can be seen in Appendix
11.2.1. It is assumed that the slope of the sides in the trapeze are dependent on the depth. The
relationship is visualised in Figure 6, where b1 is the width of the bottom of the trench, b2 is
the width of sides of the trench, and d is the depth. In DS 475:2012 it is stated that for pipe
trenches on unpaved ground, the slope of the sides should be broad enough to secure work safety.
To satisfy this requirement, the relationship between the depth, d, and b2 is based on estimations
from drainage engineer Jesper Madsen. For example if d = 1, then b2=0.5, and if d = 4, then b2 = 4.
In the case that the user chooses a separate sewer system, their pipe trenches will overlap to a certain
degree. In order not to double count the soil excavated, a proxy for this overlap was calculated
using Case 1 as an example of two pipes overlapping. Case 1 made three stretches of a separate
sewer system, and Stretch 1 was made with few optimisations and is therefore deemed as a typical
drainage project. Case 1 is explained further in Section 5.1. Figure 7 shows the concept behind
the calculation, and the detailed version can be found in Appendix 11.3.1, Sheet ”Jord”. Stretch
1 covered depths from 0.6 m up to 4.6 m, which means that all ratios are represented in this
calculation. The pipes used were of DN 600 and DN 200 giving a bottom width, b1 of 1.5 and 0.8
m respectively. The distance between the pipes were based on estimates from Jesper, and set as a
constant to 1.5 m from the middle to middle of each pipe trench (Madsen, 2024). The overlap was
calculated for the whole stretch and an average was found as a percentage overlap out of the total
26
==PAGE:37==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
area of the two trapezes. It was found that the overlap corresponds to 26% of the total area, which
is used in the screening tool. The same is done for the top soil and total width of the whole pipe
trench, where an overlap of 34% was found and used in the screening tool.
Figure 7: Conceptual drawing of two pipe trenches and their overlap in a separate sewer system.
Rd, Rb1, and Rb2 is the depth, the width of the bottom, and the width of the sides of the rainwater
pipe trench, respectively. Sd, Sb1, and Sb2 is the depth, the width of the bottom, and the width
of the sides of the trench for the sewage pipe, respectively. Ob is the width of the triangle forming
the overlap, and Od is the depth of the triangle. The points E, F, and G denotes the intersections
between the two pipe trenches.
When the pipe trench is excavated, the top soil layer is assumed to be 30 cm deep and will always
be reused if no road is being build on top. The pipe displaces an amount of soil, and 20% of the
excavated soil is assumed to be displaced by gravel used around the pipe (Madsen, 2024). The
remaining space can be filled with either new gravel or reused soil, depending on the fraction, the
user chooses to reuse in the pipe trench, see Figure 6.
The user also chooses what to do with the remaining soil. The first option is to dispose of it, denoted
”Bortskaffelse” in the screening tool. The already available process ”Jord opgrave over vand blød
bortskaffe” in InfraLCA transports and disposes soil 25 km away. With the current setup, the user
cannot change this distance in the screening tool. The other option is localized surface deposition,
or local reintegration of soil, denoted ”Lokal indbygning” in the screening tool. Here, the surplus
soil is integrated on the surface on site. The process ”Jord opgrave til depot over vand blød” and
”Jord indbygge fra depot” is used with a transport distance of 2 km.
The different types of pipes available in the screening tool can be seen in Table 4. The dimension
of pipes is denoted DN, which is the inside diameter in millimetres (mm). All plastic pipes up
to DN 1200 are used directly from InfraLCA, which already contains many different pipes with
different plastic materials, and different dimensions. The last two, large diameter corrugated plastic
pipes (DN 1800 and 2500) are ”Weholite”-pipes from the manufacturer Uponor (Holder and Udas,
2022). All concrete pipes are IBF pipes. From DN 600-1600 standard unarmed concrete pipes with
27
==PAGE:38==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
individual EPDs for emission factors are used, whereas the larger pipes are based on an EPD for
armed concrete pipes (Brandt et al., 2022).

Manholes both in concrete or plastic are also available in different dimensions. The three plastic
manholes, DN 315, 400, and 600 are used directly from the InfraLCA process ”Plastik brønd”. The
concrete manholes are calculated by weight, and their emissions are based on the specific manhole
EPD from the manufacturer IBF (Larsen, Borg, Greibe and Hauan, 2024). More information about
the calculations of manholes in the screening tool can be found in Appendix 11.2.4
4.1.3
Ditch
The next category is Ditch, denoted ”Grøft” in the Danish screening tool. A ditch is used to trans-
port water on the surface. The calculation covers the excavation and transportation of soil, and the
production of a potential membrane. The purpose of the membrane is to avoid infiltration of water
to the ground below the ditch. The volume of transported water depends on the length, depth
and width of the ditch, which are all decided by the user, together with the choice of membrane.
The user can, as in the Pipe category, choose to dispose the surplus soil or to reintegrate it in the
surroundings near the project.
The calculations are inspired by previous calculations by Emilie Berg Hansen from WSP. An ex-
ample can be found in Appendix 11.2.2. A conceptual drawing of the cross section of the ditch can
28
==PAGE:39==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
be seen in Figure 8. It shows the triangular hole for the water (blue), the membrane (grey) and the
soil (brown) which is excavated and reintegrated. This cross section is multiplied with the chosen
length to calculate the needed soil excavation and material amounts. The user can choose to not
have a membrane, and in this case, the only processes included in the calculation are the excavation
of soil, and transport of soil to disposal or local reintegration. If the user chooses a membrane,
30 cm of extra soil will be excavated, placed 10 m away from the ditch, and lastly reintegrated on
top of the membrane. In this calculation, the membrane is assumed to cover 2/3 of the sides of
the ditch, and its volume is assumed to be negligible (as described in Emilies example in Appendix
11.2.2).
It is possible for the user to choose a plastic- or a bentonite membrane. Both are laid directly
on the ground to avoid infiltration of water and thereby avoid contamination of the groundwater.
A plastic membrane is a thin plastic sheet which is completely waterproof.
The chosen plastic
membrane is an Ethylene Propylene Diene Monomer (EPDM) membrane with a thickness of 1.2 mm
(Innovaci´o and Alonso, 2018). A bentonite membrane is a plastic textile with the compressed clay
material bentonite. The membrane used in the screening tool is a bentonite-geotextile membrane
(Tomaszewska et al., 2020).
Figure 8: Conceptual drawing of the cross section of a ditch, which shows the width (b) and the
depth (d) of the final ditch volume, along with the membrane (grey) with a 30 cm layer of soil
(brown).
4.1.4
Retention Basins
The next category is retention basins for storage of storm water, in the screening tool called ”Ma-
gasinering”. Before the start of this project, WSP had already created detailed LCAs on specific
projects related to climate adaptation for the utility company Novafos, including retention basin
projects (Novafos and WSP Denmark, 2024). Due to a time limit of this project, it was chosen
to use these projects as a crude estimation of different types of retention basins, even though this
makes the calculations less flexible. The background data for the retention basins and assumptions
from WSP can be found in Appendix 11.2.3.
The screening tool contains four different types of retention basins: dry basin, wet basin, pipe basin,
and cassette basin. The wet basin functions as a lake, because of the constant wet volume, and has
extra room for retention of water. A bentonite membrane in the lower part of the basin secures a
constant volume of water in the basin. The dry basin is similar to the wet basin, except that is
29
==PAGE:40==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
only holds water during rain events. In this case a plastic membrane is installed to avoid seepage
of polluted rainwater in the ground, and a water brake helps control the flow of water. The pipe
basin and cassette basin both stores the water below ground. In this case, they are both covered by
topsoil. The pipe basin in the screening tool consists of large plastic pipes surrounded by base course
to hold them in place. The cassette basin are composed of several plastic cassettes surrounded by
a geo textile to keep out dirt particles, and only allow water to flow in the holes (Novafos and WSP
Denmark, 2024).
The details about each project can be found in Appendix 11.2.3. Table 5 shows the needed amount
of material/process per m3 retention volume.
Table 5: An overview of the amounts of retention basin per m3 volume in the screening tool.

In the screening tool, the user only needs to choose their wanted type of retention basin and
the wanted retention volume. In the current version of the tool, many parameters are therefore
predefined.
4.1.5
Drainage Project
The final category is Drainage Project, denoted as ”Afvandingsprojekt” in Danish. In this category,
all of the above mentioned categories can be combined into one project. For paved grounds, the
calculations adjust the amount of pavement and gravel removed, depending on the size of the road
instead of the pipe trench, if a road is constructed on top. If the ground is unpaved, and the user
does not choose to add a road, the pipe trench will be filled with gravel and/or reused soil and top
soil. If the user adds a road, the pipe trench will leave out space for the road section. The extra
space is accounted for as less gravel for filling. If the road is larger than the pipe trench, the amount
of less gravel is based on the size of the pipe trench, and more soil is excavated for the extra road
section outside the pipe trench.
30
==PAGE:41==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
4.2
Absolute Sustainability
In the drainage project, the user can choose to make an absolute sustainability analysis. Theoreti-
cally, this option could also be added to all the other categories individually, but this was not done
for this version of the screening tool due to time restraints. The calculation of the SR is explained
in Section 3.2. In the screening tool it is visualised as a thermometer, with a limit at 1 displaying
the threshold of the SOS. The user gives an input of the cost and lifetime of the project. If the user
does not know the lifetime, a default value of 50 years is used.
4.3
Aesthetics and Communication of Results
The results of the screening tool are displayed in different ways. As the sector often mainly focuses
on the life cycle stages A1-A5, the first figure is a bar chart of the impacts from these stages, split
into predefined categories, like pavement and soil handling. Further down a full bar chart with
all the life cycle stages A-D and all processes (using the process names in InfraLCA) is displayed,
but this is more relevant for people with some LCA background. As it is possible to add up to 3
scenarios, a bar chart comparing all the impacts from the scenarios is also displayed. Resource use
is shown as a bar chart in terms of tonnes gravel. In order to relate amounts of CO2-eq emissions
to something tangible, the total impact of the projects are also given in other carbon equivalents,
like kilos of beef or number of flights to New Work from Denmark. An overview of these equivalents
can be seen in Appendix 11.2.5.
4.4
Optimizations
Optimisation options are added to the screening tool as a source of inspiration for project planners.
An overview of these are given here.
4.4.1
Electric Machinery
Electric machines on the construction site are added as an optimisation option in the screening tool.
Based on estimates from Søren Gabriel from WSP, it is believed that the direct emissions from elec-
tric machines are 10% of the emissions from traditional machinery running on diesel (Gabriel, 2024).
If the user chooses to optimize, the installation in A5 for excavation work is therefore lowered to
10% of its original value. This applies to installation for soil handling, pipe trench filling, base
course, and subbase gravel.
In future versions of the tool, electric trucks for transport of materials instead of diesel trucks,
could be an option, although these are not yet common in Denmark. The Danish Council for Green
Transition (R˚adet for Grøn Omstilling) expects that 1/4 of the trucks in Denmark will be electric in
2035 (R˚adet for Grøn Omstilling, 2024). A large part of the emissions from the projects stems from
the transport module (A4), and choosing an electric truck could potentially decrease the overall
impact.
4.4.2
Recycled Gravel
In 2023, the total extraction of sand, gravel and stone from gravel pits on land in Denmark was
26.7 mil. m3, and the amount of marine aggregates was 9.7 mil. m3 (Danmarks Statistik, 2024).
It is expected that the overall use of materials will be increasing, and that the share of marine
gravel materials will be rising, due to a limited source on land (Danske Regioner, 2020; Region
Sjaelland and NIRAS, 2018). This lack of resources leads to increasing prices, and means that the
31
==PAGE:42==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
gravel will sometimes have to be imported from other parts of Denmark further away, or even other
countries, which increases the price and the CO2-eq emissions from transport (Dagens Byggeri,
2024). An alternative is to use recycled gravel materials. Gravel from other projects can be reused
directly, or after a simple sorting. It is also possible to use waste products from the building sector,
e.g. tile/bricks, or concrete, or old asphalt, as also mentioned in Section 4.1.1. These materials
are crushed to a finer product which can substitute subbase gravel or base course (Kwed´eris and
Nørg˚ard, 2024).
If the user chooses recycled gravel as an optimization, it is assumed that the
production (A1-A3) of material is 0, assuming that the gravel is reused directly from another
project and not consists of crushed bricks, asphalt, or tiles.
4.4.3
Sustainable Concrete Pipes
IBF has developed concrete pipes with reduced emissions called Eco2 (Larsen, Hauan, Mølgaard
and Borg, 2024). The carbon footprint from concrete products is large due to the use of cement
(Purton, 2024). In the Eco2 pipes, the cement has been replaced with a product called Geoprime®,
which is a waste product from steel production (Larsen, Hauan, Mølgaard and Borg, 2024). The
EPD for Eco2 pipes (ø500) is compared with the normal ø500 concrete pipe (Larsen, Anderson,
Mølgaard and Hauan, 2024). The pipes have the same dimensions, weight, and overall life cycle.
The only module that varies in the results is the production A1-A3, where the Eco2 pipe has 40%
lower emissions than the normal pipe (Larsen, Hauan, Mølgaard and Borg, 2024). This percentage
is used as a possible optimization for all concrete pipes in the screening tool, but not the concrete
manholes or sewer overflow, and is only added in the production stage A1-A3.
4.4.4
Sustainable Plastic Pipes
Plastic pipes can be produced with a mixture of virgin and recycled plastics, which saves resources
and decreases the amount of CO2-eq emissions. Different stakeholders came together to create a
Danish pilot-project for developing the ”supply pipes of the future” (DANVA, 2022). The project
groups consisted of different utility companies (Lemvig Vand A/S as the leading one), the Danish
plastic industry (NPG Danmark), the Technological Institute, ”Klimatorium”, and Plastix A/S in
Lemvig. They tested both pressure (DN 110) - and gravitational (DN 200) pipes with a smooth
surface, and added different percentages of secondary plastic material. They found that it was
possible to decrease CO2-eq emissions from gravitational pipes with 30-70% (DANVA, 2022).
Uponor also developed the ”Ultra Rib 2 Blue”-pipes. These corrugated PP plastic pipe consists
of both virgin and bioplastic materials. The bioplastic is a recycled material, which stems from
feedstocks, derived from waste- and residue vegetable oils. Uponor claims that this reduces the
carbon footprint with up to 70% (Uponor, 2024). The Ultra Rib 2 blue pipes are produced with a
maximum diameter of 560 mm (Wang and Charndramathy, 2022).
Based on the Ultra Rib 2 Blue pipe and the findings from the sustainable-pipe project, a middle
value of 50% is chosen as the factor for more sustainable plastic pipes in the screening tool. This is
added as an optimization option for all smooth and corrugated plastic pipes. The 50% reduction is
only affecting the A1-A3 module. Although it seems possible to add recycled materials to pipes with
larger diameters than 400 mm (e.g. HDPE Culvert Pipe from Cotterill Civils in the UK (Cotterill
Civils, 2024)), it has not been possible to find proper documentation for the impacts. Therefore
the recycled material optimization for larger pipes is uncertain, and could be further investigated
in future work.
32
==PAGE:43==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
5
Case 1: Separate Sewage Stretches
The first case to be analysed is a project by the utility company KLAR Forsyning and will be
described in further detail in the following sections.
5.1
Case Description
KLAR Forsyning is the utility company of four municipalities: Køge, Greve, Solrød and Stevns.
Their responsibilities include waste management, heat distribution and water supply (KLAR Forsyn-
ing, 2024). In 2019, a construction of a new area in Køge North (Køge N) started (KLAR Forsyning,
2019). Part of the construction consisted of three different stretches with separate sewer systems.
For each stretch, KLAR Forsyning made different optimisations in regard to reductions in emis-
sions. These stretches will be denoted Stretch 1, 2, and 3 in this report, and analysed using different
degrees of detailed data. Project leader Bettina Precht Simonsen from KLAR Forsyning and Den-
nis Johansen from Tscherning A/S have been helpful in providing the needed data about the project.
The project was done on land that previously had been used for agriculture, which meant that soil
could be moved around freely without being tested for pollutants. A share of the excavated soil
from the pipe trench was reused as filling in the pipe trench while surplus soil, displaced by the
pipe, was locally reintegrated on site. The machines used on the site were monitored to keep track
of their fuel use and the amount of time the engine was idling. This only applied for the excavation
machines and dumpers, which contribute with roughly 80-90% of the total fuel use on site. Every 50
to 80 meters, concrete manholes were installed. (Simonsen and Johansen, 2024). For each stretch
KLAR Forsyning focused on optimizing the processes to decrease emissions from the project. The
optimisations at each stretch build on top on each other, meaning that an optimisation in Stretch
1 is carried into Stretch 2. An overview of this is given in the following sections.
5.1.1
Stretch 1
The construction of Stretch 1 was a ”typical” separate sewage system in terms of the excavation
and material use. The stretch of the rainwater pipe was split into three different types of pipes:
concrete pipes in dimensions DN 600 and DN 700, and a plastic pipe of DN 560. The sewage water
pipe was a plastic pipe of DN 200. Four manholes with a diameter of 1500 mm and eight smaller
manholes with a diameter of 1250 mm were used. For a part of the stretch, the constructors needed
to add soil to elevate the terrain. Instead of buying extra soil, they used the excess soil from other
parts of the project on site, meaning that no new soil was used (Simonsen and Johansen, 2024).
Materials for the construction were delivered at the end of the stretch, and transported by a wheel
loader to the place where it needed to be used.
5.1.2
Stretch 2
For Stretch 2, KLAR Forsyning, changed the type of pipe for the sewage water from regular
Polypropylene (PP) plastic to ”Ultra Rib 2 Blue” pipes produced by Uponor with DN 200. These
pipes are partly made from bio-plastic, derived from waste- and residue vegetable oils, which reduces
its carbon footprint (Uponor, 2024). They also changed the rainwater pipes, from concrete to plastic
with DN 560. Plastic pipes weigh less than concrete pipes, and they therefore require less heavy
machine work to get into place, and has a lower impact per kilometre transported. Only manholes
with a diameter of 1250 mm were used, instead of 1500 mm. The entrepreneurs used machine
control to optimize the excavation profile, which reduced the amount of soil needed to be moved,
33
==PAGE:44==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
and thereby. They also focused on decreasing the time the machines spend idling. Furthermore,
the pipes where placed closer to each other, thereby decreasing the amount of soil needed to be
excavated. The wastewater pipe was placed deeper than the rainwater pipe, because the wastewater
pipe needs to have a higher slope. When moving the soil, pipes, manholes, and other materials with
the dumper, they made sure to move it directly to the place where it had to be integrated at the
site in order decrease transport (Simonsen and Johansen, 2024).
5.1.3
Stretch 3
In Stretch 3, the digging practices had become even more precise, which meant that less soil had to
be lifted from the pipe trench. The excavator had a special ditch bucket shovel with a small square
on the tip, which shaped the pipe trench to fit better. All the soil was used to heighten the terrain
directly over the pipe trench, which meant that the excavator could put the soil directly next to
the pipe trench whilst digging. In Stretch 3 there was therefore almost no use of the dumper for
transporting the materials around the site. In parts of the stretch, they used a plastic rainwater
pipe with a smaller diameter of 315 mm instead of 560 mm. This allowed for less soil excavation,
since less space is needed in the pipe trench, and a lower material use for the pipe. In Stretch 3 the
amount of gravel around the pipes was reduced to the absolute minimum, and some of the gravel
was reused gravel, delivered from another project (Simonsen and Johansen, 2024).
5.2
Functional Unit
For Case 1, the function of the system is to provide a steady flow of rainwater and sewage water from
one place to another that can be monitored through manholes along the way. The functional unit
is therefore defined as: The construction of 1 meter of rainwater and wastewater transportation,
with a frequency of manholes that complies with Danish regulation, in Køge N, for 50 years.
5.3
System Boundary
Figure 9 shows the process tree for Case 1. Materials for components (pipes and manholes) and
raw materials for the construction and the transport of these materials to the site are all included
in the system boundary. Soil excavated from the site is reintegrated directly at the project site,
and no new soil is therefore used. Petrol and diesel accounts for all transport and construction
installation processes on site, including soil excavation. The production of machines, dumpers, and
other equipment used at the construction site are outside the system boundary. This is due to the
assumption, that such element also will be used at other construction sites and therefore contribute
negligibly to the overall impact. Lastly, the reuse of materials is also included.
34
==PAGE:45==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Figure 9: Process tree for Case 1. The dashed line is the system boundary, the grey boxes shows
the materials used for the project, brown boxes shows components in the project, green is transport
and installation, and blue is EoL stages. The white boxes are outside the system boundary and
therefore not accounted for.
5.4
Detailed LCA
Within the system boundary, three LCA studies were conducted in order to compare results with
different degrees of detail in the input. The first analysis was the Detailed LCA. The Detailed
LCA was calculated using InfraLCA, where we added relevant processes, based on product-specific
EPDs, manually to increase the level of detail.
Based on the data by KLAR Forsyning, a Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) for each stretch was made.
The LCIs for Stretch 1, 2, and 3 can be found in Appendix 11.3.10. The LCI contains an overview
of the product type, the main material, the diameter of the manholes or the pipes, the amount, the
specific name of the process used in InfraLCA, documentation of the emission factor, and the data
quality. A detailed description of the criteria for the rating of data quality is found in Section 3.1.3.
The data, calculations, and InfraLCA files can be found in Appendix 11.3.1 and 11.3.2.
5.5
Standard LCA
An analysis of Case 1 using only standard options and products in InfraLCA was made, and is
denoted the Standard LCA in this analysis. It was chosen to make a separate calculation of the
excavated soil amounts due to difficulties in assessing the scoping of the A5 module for pipes in
InfraLCA. KLAR Forsyning provided the transported soil amounts for Stretch 2 and 3, whereas the
soil for Stretch 1 was calculated as described in Section 4.1.2, as two partly overlapping trapezes,
summed up along the entirity if Strech 1. The full calculation can be seen in Appendix 11.3.1, Sheet
”Jord”.
35
==PAGE:46==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
In order to take the reuse of soil into account in InfraLCA, it was assumed that the soil is ex-
cavated and transported to a depot, where it is stored until the project is ready to have the soil
reintegrated. The excavation and reintegration is calculated assuming a 500 m distance both back
and forth from the depot for Stretch 1 and 2, and a 10 m distance for Stretch 3. This is due to
information that soil was moved and reused directly next to the pipe trench in Stretch 3, but in
Stretch 1 and 2, soil was integrated in other areas within the project (Simonsen and Johansen, 2024).
In InfraLCA there are a select choice of pipe- and manhole dimensions. For concrete, the possible
diameters are: DN 400, 1000 and 1600. For plastic, there are three different plastic materials: PE
(smooth pipes), PP (smooth and corrugated pipes) and PVC (smooth pipes). The pipes used in
the calculations are PP for both the smooth- and the corrugated pipe. The diameter options for
both are DN 200, 300, 400, 500, 600, and 800. An overview of the chosen concrete and plastic pipes
can be seen in Table 6. Some pipes are overestimated, and some are underestimated. The manhole
size in the Standard LCA is DN 1000, although the majority of manholes used were DN 1250, and
the rest were DN 1500, and they are therefore also underestimated. A full LCI for the Standard
LCA can be found in Appendix 11.3.11, and the InfraLCA files with the calculations are found in
Appendix 11.3.2.
5.6
Screening Tool LCA
As described in Section 4, a screening tool has been developed as part of this report. The type
of project chosen in the screening tool is Pipes (Ledninger), described in Section 4.1.2. In inputs
chosen as inputs to the screening tool each of the three stretches can be found in Appendix 11.3.13.
The LCI for the Screening tool LCA can be found in Appendix 11.3.12.
Some elements in the case had to be grouped together in order to fit in the screening tool. An
overview can be found in Table 6. This includes the part of the stretch of plastic rainwater pipe
in Stretch 1, which is assumed to be concrete with DN 600. All manholes in Stretch 1 are also
assumed to be DN 1250 instead of the four manholes with a diameter of 1500 mm. When using the
optimisation feature for more sustainable plastic pipes, it is only possible to change all pipes at the
same time, even though it was only the small DN 200 pipes for wastewater that were actually in a
more sustainable material. The same applies for gravel optimisation which is applied to all gravel,
not only part of the gravel as is the case in Stretch 3.

5.7
Results and Discussion
The results from the LCA of each of the three stretches, calculated using the Detailed, Standard,
and Screening Tool method, are presented here. Figure 10 shows the result divided into the lifecycle
stages A-D. An overview of the calculations of the results can be seen in Appendix 11.3.3.
37
==PAGE:48==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Figure 10: The results of three different LCA calculations of Case 1. They are divided according
to the life cycle stages (A-D). Left: the Detailed LCA, middle: the Standard LCA, and right: the
Screening Tool LCA.
For all three calculation methods, there is a decline in impacts from Stretch 1 to Stretch 3. The
decline is most apparent in the Detailed LCA. The screening tool and the standard version of In-
fraLCA give fewer options for changing inputs, and this lack of detail gives the smaller difference
between the stretches.
The overall decline in impact happens due to the changes described in
Sections 5.1.1, 5.1.2, and 5.1.3, which affects the following parameters: type of pipe material, the
dimension for pipes and manholes, and the handling of soil.
Changing from concrete to plastic pipes between Stretch 1 and 2, decrease emissions in A1-A3, as
plastic pipes have a lower emission factor than concrete. Furthermore, concrete pipes are heavier
than plastic pipes and therefore require more energy during transportation in A4. Decreasing the
dimension of the pipes and manholes also decreases the material needed, and thereby the emissions
associated with production and transportation. In Stretch 1, 4 of the 12 manholes have a dimension
of 1500 mm and the rest have a dimension of 1250 mm, whereas only the smaller dimension of 1250
mm manholes were used in Stretch 2 and 3, resulting in fewer emissions than in Stretch 1. In Stretch
3, a smaller dimension for parts of the rainwater pipe was chosen, and therefore emissions from the
production of pipes decrease even further in Stretch 3. All of this is captured in the Detailed LCA.
The change in impact from concrete pipes to plastic pipes between Stretch 1 and 2 does not show
clearly in the resulting emission in the Standard LCA. This is due to the limited dimensions avail-
able in InfraLCA for concrete pipes. In the Detailed LCA, concrete pipes of DN 600 and DN 700
38
==PAGE:49==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
were used, while concrete pipes of DN 400 were used in the Standard LCA. In Stretch 2 in the
Detailed LCA, plastic pipes of DN 560 were used, while the Standard LCA used DN 600. Further-
more, the plastic pipe used in the Detailed LCA was Ultra Rib 2 Blue, which has a smaller carbon
footprint than regular plastic pipes. The standard version of InfraLCA does not include plastic
pipes from bio-plastic materials, and this optimisation is therefore not visible in the Standard LCA
calculation. The emissions of concrete pipes are therefore underestimated in the Standard LCA in
Stretch 1, while plastic pipes are overestimated in Stretch 2, which in turn results in a no stagnation
in emissions from Stretch 1 to Stretch 2 in A1-A3.
In the screening tool there is an option for choosing plastic pipes partly made up of recycled plastic,
resembling the Ultra Rib 2 Blue in both Stretch 2 and 3. As mentioned in Section 4.4.4, a 50%
decrease in emissions from A1-A3 is assumed for the optimised plastic pipe in the screening tool.
When using this optimisation, it is applied to all plastic pipes, and it is therefore expected that the
resulting emissions will be underestimated, when it is actually only a part of the pipes that are of
more sustainable material. Emissions from production processes, A1-A3, in Stretch 2 do decrease
compared to Stretch 1 but seem very similar for the Screening Tool and the Detailed LCA. Further
investigation shows that emissions from all plastic pipes are 22% smaller in the Screening Tool,
compared to the total impact from plastic pipes in the Detailed LCA, but that the Screening Tool
LCA has higher emissions from use of gravel in A1-A3 compared to the Detailed LCA.
In Stretch 3, reused gravel is used. In the Screening Tool LCA reused gravel has an impact of 0
in A1-A3, as it is assumed to be reused directly from another project, see Section 4.4.2. As with
the plastic pipe, this optimization is applied to all gravel used in the stretch, even though approxi-
mately half of the gravel was new materials in reality. The emissions from the production of gravel
is therefore underestimated in the Screening Tool LCA compared to the Detailed LCA.
Soil handling is reflected in the petrol and diesel use in the Detailed LCA in A5, and the reduction
is clear, especially from Stretch 2 to Stretch 3. The resulting emissions in the Standard LCA are
lower than both the Detailed and the Screening Tool LCA in all stretches respectively, and it seems
that it is mainly the smaller impacts in A5, that result in this difference. The Standard LCA is able
to catch parts of the decrease by changing the transport distance of the soil to the depot, but the
overall emission in A5 is smaller in the Standard LCA than in both the Detailed and the Screening
Tool. The screening tool uses the same process as InfraLCA for soil excavation and integration,
but there is a difference in the amount excavated and the transport distance to the depot. In the
Standard LCA, the transport distance varies from 500 m in Stretch 1 and 2, to 10 m in Stretch
3, while this is fixed at 2 km in the Screening Tool. The amount of soil excavated is on average
1.1-1.5 times larger in the Screening tool LCA compared to the Standard LCA, where the amounts
were based on the actual amounts from the project. This indicates, that the screening tool overes-
timates the amount of soil excavated, even in Stretch 1, which should be the conservative stretch.
It should be noted that the actual amounts of soil excavated in Stretch 1 were not available, so this
is an estimated number, as mentioned in Section 5.5 and is therefore more uncertain. The larger
impact from A5 in the Screening Tool LCA is therefore due to differences in transport distance
and amounts of soil excavated. This indicates, at least for this specific case, that InfraLCA might
underestimate emissions associated with construction installation processes when comparing to the
actual emissions seen in the Detailed LCA. In the screening LCA, there is no change in A5, since
it is currently not possible for the user to change the amount of excavated soil or the transport
distance. There is an option of reuse and local reintegration of soil, but this was an optimisation
used in all three stretches, and therefore no difference in A5 is seen in the Screening Tool LCA.
39
==PAGE:50==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
The transport stage, A4, is very similar in both the the Standard and the Detailed LCA. The
transport distance in the Detailed LCA is an average of distances from the construction site to the
production sites and different gravel pits. In the Screening Tool LCA, the transport stage (A4)
has much higher emissions. This is mainly due to the conservative default of having 20% gravel
filling in the pipe trench. The Screening Tool LCA uses 3.3 times more gravel in Stretch 1, and 8.6
times more in Stretch 3 than in the actual project, which shows that the Screening Tool seems to
overestimate the amount of gravel needed, also in the conservative stretch. Most emissions from
gravel are from transportation, and in the screening tool the transport distance is 132 km, where
the Detailed has a distance of 94 km, which also leads to higher emissions in the Screening Tool LCA.
In general, emissions from stages C and D have rather low values. Most materials used in the case
are expected to have a long lifetime, many are up to 100 years, and therefore the processes in the
C and D module are covered in much uncertainty as they are scenario-based.
Figure 11 shows the resulting emissions from stages A1-A5, split into defined categories.
Figure 11: The results of three different LCA calculations of Case 1. They are divided according to
the categories Gravel and sand (A1-A3), Pipes and manholes (A1-A3) and Transport and installation
(A4-A5). The results does not take into account the C and D module. Left: the Detailed LCA,
middle: the Standard LCA, and right: the Screening Tool LCA.
Transport and installation (A4-A5) is a large contributor to the overall emissions in almost all
stretches and using all methods, followed by Pipes and manholes (A1-A3). The major contribution
to climate change from gravel and sand comes from the transport stage, as they are heavy materials,
and emissions from production of gravel and sand seem negligible. The importance of different inputs
are investigated further in the sensitivity analysis in Section 5.9.

5.8
Uncertainty and Distribution of Data
In the Detailed LCA, several EPDs have been used in order to retrieve emission factors for specific
products. This section will give an overview of the main uncertainties and limitations of the emission
factors used in the study.
5.8.1
Concrete Pipes
Figure 12 gives an overview of the concrete pipes used to create an average of emissions in In-
fraLCA. The figure shows the existing concrete pipes in InfraLCA in blue nuances, based on EPDs
from Dansk Beton Afløbsforening, Gammelrand (Larsen and Merlin, 2021), and the Swedish com-
pany S:t Eriks AB (Nilsson and Lindman, 2020). Based on these EPDs, a function was made to
compare with the emission factor of the concrete pipe used in the Detailed LCA and in the Screening
Tool LCA. Emissions from stages A1-A3 for the concrete pipes in InfraLCA of DN 400 deviate with
less than 5 kg CO2-eq per m pipe, while concrete pipes of DN 1000 show a difference between Dansk
Beton Afløbsforening and S:t Eriks of almost 100 kg CO2-eq per m pipe. The EPD from Dansk
Beton Afløbsforening is calculated as an average of the three biggest suppliers of concrete pipes in
Denmark: Gammelrand, IBF og RC Beton, covering around 97% of the Danish market (Larsen
and Merlin, 2021). They state that the average is weighted and can vary up to 19% depending on
the producer. As Gammelrand is both included in the average from Dansk Beton Afløbsforening
and in the average generated for DN 400 concrete pipes in InfraLCA, it can be discussed whether
it is representative to include this twice in an average. Even so, as the final data points of the
existing EPDs are already very similar, and it would therefore probably not change the final result
significantly to omit them.
IBF Uarmeret Betonrør is the data used in the Detailed LCA. They used pipes in sizes DN 600
and DN 700, but the EPD available was DN 500, that aligned with the actual pipes used (Larsen,
Anderson, Mølgaard and Hauan, 2024; Simonsen and Johansen, 2024). It was therefore converted
into impact per tonnes instead of meters for the use of the calculations. IBF Uarmeret Betonrør
aligns quite well with the linear regression of the data points from InfraLCA’s average. The specific
EPD from IBF align the detailed analysis with the actual product used from IBF, but an approxi-
mate value could have been well achieved using the data already in InfraLCA. A problem with using
standard values from InfraLCA is, that only diameters of 400, 1000, and 1600 mm are available, and
the user is therefore forced to choose the one closest to it. In the screening tool more dimensions
were added, based on EPDs from IBF as explained in Section 4.1.2. In Case 1, concrete pipes from
IBF with DN 600 were used, displayed on the graph as ”IBF Uarmeret ig-fodrør”. Compared to
the existing values in InfraLCA and the value used in the Detailed, it has a lower emission factor.
The EPDs used in the Screening Tool are newer compared to the ones used in InfraLCA, thereby
a higher temporal representativeness. This lower emission factor could be due to technological ad-
vancements or technical differences in the product that requires more background knowledge than
we currently posses. Therefore, it is good to be aware that the screening tool might underestimate
the impacts from concrete pipes. The calculation of the distribution can be found in Appendix
11.3.4.
41
==PAGE:52==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Figure 12: Overview of the emission factors for concrete pipes (A1-A3) already existing in InfraLCA
in blue nuances, the Detailed LCA in green (IBF Uarmeret Betonrør), and the Screening Tool LCA
in red (IBF Uarmeret ig-fodrør) of Case 1, dependent of the pipe diameter.
Some concrete pipes, e.g. EPD NEPD-5624-4923-DK from IBF Larsen, Anderson, Mølgaard and
Hauan (2024), report negative emissions in the use phase, B1, due to reabsorption of CO2. This
is not accounted for in this report, and was deemed negligible in terms of screening purposes. The
investigation can be seen in Appendix 11.3.9.
5.8.2
Plastic Pipes
Many different types of plastic pipes were used in Case 1. The EPDs used for plastic pipes from
Pipelife, NEPD-4386-3617-EN, NEPD-4323-3558-EN, and NEPD-4434-3699-EN, only had values for
the production phase, A1-A3, as they assume that the pipes will remain in the ground indefinitely,
and therefore the stages C and D are not accounted for. In Copenhagen, the first sewer system was
laid in 1859, and parts of this first sewer system is still in use today. This was made of bricks, but
still the shows the longevity of sewer systems (Andersen, 2012). It may therefore be a reasonable
assumption that many sewage pipes stay in the ground for a very long time. Even so, the pipe
from Uponor (Wang and Charndramathy, 2022) does include the stages, C and D, and the final re-
sult might therefore underestimate the impact when these are not included in the other plastic pipes.
Figure 13 shows the distribution of emissions from plastic pipes, split into the categories ”Corru-
gated” and ”Smooth”. The distribution of the boxplot is based on data from InfraLCA, while the
dots represent the specific EPDs used in the Detailed LCA. It can be seen that all pipe types are
within the values from InfraLCA in stage A1-A3 except the Ultra Rib 2 Blue pipe, partly made
from bioplastic (Wang and Charndramathy, 2022). As a full LCI is not available in the EPD, it
is therefore not clear how they have accounted for the environmental impacts tied to the use of
renewable plastic.
42
==PAGE:53==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
In the waste handling stage, C1-C4, values are generally quite small, ranging from 0-0.8 kg CO2-
eq per kg. plastic pipe, compared to the production stages A1-A3, with emissions of 0.8-2.4 kg
CO2-eq per kg. plastic pipe. Emissions from corrugated pipes are generally smaller than for the
smooth pipes in C1-C4. It is not expected that the dismantling processes are very different for
corrugated and smooth pipes, so the larger values cannot be explained without further information
of the calculations behind the EPDs. Generally it is concluded that the C stage for plastic pipes
are rather small compared to other life cycle stages. Even though there is uncertainty connected to
the values, they do not seem to contribute largely to the final result. It is therefore not essential
to fine-tune these values, but rather accept the indication they give to the overall picture. As the
overall values for plastic pipes align well with pipes used in KLAR Forsyning, it is assessed that the
standard plastic pipes (DN 32-1400) in InfraLCA can be used in the screening tool without further
concern. The calculation of the boxplots can be found in Appendix 11.3.4.

(b) Boxplots of GHG emissions from stage C1-C4.

32-400 mm, respectively. The boxplots shows the range of data used in InfraLCA, while the dots
represent specific data used for the Detailed analysis of Case 1.
5.8.3
Gravel
The origin of the gravel used in Case 1 is unknown. Only one of the companies who delivered the
materials have available EPDs (NCC a/s), but the exact gravel pit is unknown. Instead it was
chosen to calculate our own average of different EPDs on gravel, as processes for Pebble 8/16 and
sand was lacking in InfraLCA. All the EPDs used are from Danish gravel pits, and the majority
is from Zealand where the project is taking place, ensuring high geographical representativeness.
The EPDs have calculated stage C differently, and most of them have chosen not to include all
of the C stages, since the raw materials can be reused directly. They all have C1, which is the
”deconstruction”, or removing the materials from the ground.
In InfraLCA there is already an average emission factor on subbase gravel and base course, and
an average for other types of gravel was therefore added, also to the screening tool. Table 7 shows
the abbreviations and translations used for the gravel types in this report. The distribution of the
data input is analysed in order to see how much they vary, which is seen in Figure 14. EPDs S-P-
05225 (Løng), S-P-05225 (Svebølle), MD-22000-DA, and S-P-05226 has been used both in our own
calculation and in InfraLCA. Information about the EPDs used for the calculation of Pebble 8/16
and Sand can be found in Appendix 11.3.6, and the full calculation is found in Appendix 11.3.5.
The calculation of the boxplots can be found in Appendix 11.3.4.

Figure 14: Distribution of emissisons for differnt gravel types used in the Screening Tool, the
Standard, and the Detailed LCA. See Table 7 for name convention.
Figure 14 shows that the average of subbase gravel and base course from our own calculations is
slightly lower than the average from InfraLCA. This is mainly due to EPD S-P-04034, which is
based on marine extraction of materials, while the other EPDs are of materials extracted from land.
Our own calculation includes one EPD, S-P-02081, from NCC on Avedøre which is a terminal for
marine aggregates (NCC Industry Nordic AB, 2023). Statistics on the Danish aggregate extraction
shows that in 2022 extraction on land was about 31.8 mio. m3 and marine extraction was 11.3 mil.
m3 (Danmarks Statistik, 2024). To make a representative average of aggregates used in Denmark,
about a third of the average should therefore be based on marine extraction. This is a recommen-
dation for further development of the data input in the screening tool and InfraLCA.
For the impacts in stage C1-C4, most impacts are smaller than the impacts from A1-A3, with some
even being zero. Figure 14b shows that there is one value much higher than the rest for many of
the gravel types. This value is from EPD MD-23218-DA (Sørensen and Høibye, 2024). This EPD
includes all C-stages, C1-C4, which both includes the deconstruction of the product, transport,
treatment, and disposal. This data point might therefore be closer to the actual value than the
ones found in the other EPDs, since it accounts for all processes in the waste treatment stage. As
previously mentioned, most products within the drainage sector assumes a long life time and the
C-stage is therefore imbedded with several uncertainties in regard to its future use and emissions.
It can therefore be discussed how much weight should be put on the results from the C-stage in the
final result.
44
==PAGE:55==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
The main takeaway from this analysis is that the general distribution of the average emissions
factors of different types of gravel seems to be within the same range, with a maximum difference
of 1.7 kg CO2-eq per tonnes gravel of all averages in A1-A3, and a maximum difference of 2.3 kg
CO2-eq per tonnes gravel in the C stage. It is therefore assessed that a simplification of using only
one type of gravel, in this case subbase gravel, as filling in the pipe trench for the screening tool,
will not lead to significant differences compared to allowing to choose specific types of gravel. Even
so, the averages of the gravel types calculated here will still be used in the screening tool as options
for different types of pavements, but this is not relevant in Case 1.
5.9
Sensitivity Analysis
A sensitivity analysis was performed in order to investigate how much the final result is affected by
changes of different input parameters. The calculations can be found in Appendix 11.3.7.
5.9.1
Change in Material Input
Figure 15 shows the resulting sensitivity analysis of the Detailed LCA of Stretch 1. Some processes
are aggregated: Petrol and Diesel are combined into one parameter, all plastic pipes are combined,
and all concrete pipes are combined. Changes are varied by changing its parameter from 0.5 of
the original amount up to 1.5 of the original amount, while keeping the rest constant. This can
be seen on the x-axis, where 1 means that the parameter value is equal the same amount as in
the baseline. The y-axis gives the %-change in total CO2-eq emissions. A value of 0% means that
there is no change in the total result compared to the baseline. Figure 15 shows different lines for
each material, and the steeper the line, the bigger the change in the final output when the input
parameter changes.
Figure 15: Sensitivity analysis of inputs in Stretch 1 in the Detailed LCA of Case 1. Input param-
eters are changed ± 50%. The y-axis shows the %-change in the total emissions for Stretch 1.
45
==PAGE:56==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
The conclusions to be drawn from Figure 15 are that concrete pipes can impact the final result with
up to 18%, while Diesel and Petrol changes the result up to 15% when varying the amount with
±50%. Meanwhile, the total emissions for gravel types (Sand and Pebble 8/16) do not change more
than 8%. It is therefore more important to decrease the use of diesel, petrol, and concrete pipes
than gravel, plastic pipes, and sand in Stretch 1.
Figure 16 shows the sensitivity of the inputs in Stretch 3. Here, the same tendency can be seen
for petrol and diesel as was seen in the sensitivity analysis for Stretch 1. Diesel and petrol have a
greater sensitivity than the other inputs. In Stretch 3 no concrete pipes were used and the plastic
pipes are now the second most sensitive parameter in Stretch 3, with manholes coming in third. In
a sewage system project it therefore seems to be important first to decrease the amount of transport
and excavation, and thereby the use of diesel and petrol, and secondly to consider the material and
dimension of pipes and manholes. Even though sand and gravel does not seem to matter as much,
they still contribute to the diesel and petrol consumption.
Figure 16: Sensitivity analysis of inputs in Stretch 3 in the Detailed LCA of Case 1. Input param-
eters are changed ± 50%. The y-axis shows the %-change in the total emissions for Stretch 3.
Table 8 shows the relative sensitivity coefficient, S, for the different parameters changed in Stretch
1 and 3. The formula for calculating it can be found in Section 3.1.4, Equation 1. Results are
colour-coded with the following: Green: S>0.05, Yellow: S>0.3, and Red: S>0.5. This means
that, according to the categorisation of significance of the sensitivity parameter in Section 3.1.4,
the yellow marking has medium sensitivity, while red is highly sensitive. The green markings are
referred to as less sensitive, while the parameters with no markings are deemed negligible.

5.9.2
Change in Transport Distances
A sensitivity analysis on the A4 and A5 transport distances was performed for the input parameters
in the Screening Tool LCA. Only the transport, and not installation processes of A5, are changed.
The two processes for soil handling in this case ”Soil excavation to depot” and ”Soil reintegration
from depot” are combined into ”Soil handling”. The distance to the depot used in the Screening
Tool LCA is assumed to be 2 km in A5. The transport in A4, from production to project site, is
analysed for the gravel filling (132 km), plastic pipes (1500 km), and concrete pipes and manholes
(107 km).

The most sensitive parameter is the transport of gravel filling. According to the sensitivity coeffi-
cient in Table 9, it is medium sensitive in Stretch 1 and highly sensitive in Stretch 3. The emissions
from transport are based on the weight transported per distance, here in the unit tonnes per km
(tkm). Gravel is heavy (1.9 t/m3) and used in large amounts in the pipe stretches, and as pre-
viously mentioned, is overestimated in the screening tool 3.3-8.6 times in Case 1. The sensitivity
of transport of gravel filling is higher in Stretch 3 than 1, since the overall emissions from other
components are lower in Stretch 3.
The rest of the parameters have a low sensitivity. The second most sensitive parameter is the soil
handling, which is the transport of soil on site. Soil has a high density, which heightens the emissions
47
==PAGE:58==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
from transport. In Case 1, all soil is reused on site, which results in large quantities moved on site.
Concrete pipes and Concrete manhole have a minor or negligible sensitivity, meaning that their
transport distance might be predefined in the screening tool without significant impact on the final
result.
5.10
Absolute Sustainability
It was not possible to retrieve the construction cost for the specific stretches in Case 1 in order
to perform an absolute sustainability assessment.
But through the interview with Bettina, she
estimated that KLAR Forsyning emits about 20 tonnes CO2-eq per million DKK invested. Using
this number, a graph of the sustainability ratio, SR, was made, dependent on the lifetime of the
projects. This is seen in Figure 17. It is clear, that SR decrease with a power function with increasing
lifetime. Using the allocation methods described in Section 3.2, KLAR Forsyning is within the SOS
when their projects exceed a lifetime of about 30 years.
Figure 17: A graph displaying the relation between the sustainability ratio (SR) and the lifetime of
projects at KLAR Forsyning. SOS is the Safe Operating Space.
5.11
Conclusion in regard to the Screening Tool
Generally, it was possible to see the improvements in the three stretches using the screening tool,
but the improvement was not as apparent as in the Detailed LCA. The main difference in the
two analyses was seen in the emissions from transport, A4, which was very high in the Screening
Tool compared to the Detailed LCA. In the Screening Tool LCA, the amount of gravel is 3.3-8.6
times higher than in the Detailed LCA, which leads to significantly higher A4 emissions due to
the heaviness of gravel. On the basis of this difference in results, it can be concluded that the
screening tool is quite conservative in its use of gravel, and also in the calculation of pipe trench
size. In future versions of the tool it can be considered to allow the user to change some of these
parameters. Construction installation processes, A5, are rather constant in the Screening Tool LCA
for the three stretches, since the user has limited possibilities for changing parameters related to
installation processes. This means that optimisations, as the ones in Case 1, cannot be depicted
in the screening tool. A solution could therefore be to include an option of optimisation on soil
handling or amounts or transport of gravel. Generally, A5 processes seemed to be underestimated
48
==PAGE:59==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
in InfraLCA, and it might therefore be beneficial to further investigate if this is only specific to
this case or a general issue, as these are the processes also used in the screening tool. Diesel and
Petrol consumption in the Detailed LCA was found to be the most sensitive parameter, meaning
that optimising soil handling and installation processes have great impact in Case 1.
Furthermore, when using pipes and manholes, it is important that the user can choose the amounts
needed and differentiate between different dimensions and types of material, as this impacts the
final result significantly, based on the sensitivity analysis of the Detailed LCA. If concrete pipes are
present, reductions can be achieved by changing to plastic pipes, and even more so, if the plastic
pipes consist of more sustainable material. The screening tool applies optimisations on all pipes,
when chosen, and this might therefore lead to overestimating the acutal reductions, if only parts of
the pipes are of sustainable material. Transport distances of pipes and manholes on the other hand
seem less significant, and these might therefore be predefined in the screening tool.
49
==PAGE:60==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
6
Case 2: Combined Sewage Pipe
The exact identification of Case 2 is classified, and data that link directly to the case will therefore
not be disclosed in this report. For further information about specifications on the case, you can
contact the authors of this report.
6.1
Case Description
The objective of Case 2 is to construct a large combined sewage pipe, that can retain wastewater
in order to limit discharge to a nearby lake. In order to comply with the requirements in regard
to discharge of nutrients and pollutants to the lake, it is assessed that 5500 m3 water should be
retained in the pipes. The functional unit of the system will therefore be retaining of 5500 m3
wastewater in the specific area of Case 2 for 50 years.
This requires the dimension of the pipe to be approximately 1500 m long with 2500 mm diameter.
This type of pipe is delivered in lengths of 2, 2.5 and 3 m and are therefore quite large, which means
that a maximum of 2 pipes can be transported per truck. The first 500 m of the pipe construction
is underneath asphalt road, while the final 1000 m is below a gravel path or green area. All parts
are expected to be constructed with bracing (Plessing, 2024).
The excavators need a turning radius which requires that some of the plants in the area have to be
cleared out. Depending on how much of the road should be closed off to traffic in order to make
space for the construction installation processes, different amounts of plants should be removed. In
order to optimise on the time use and environmental impacts, all transport of soil should be limited.
This means that soil should be reused in the pipe trench in the degree possible. As a prerequisite for
this, the soil needs space to be stored (Plessing, 2024). If larger amounts of plants are removed, this
gives space for soil storage right next to the pipe trench, while the soil will be moved temporarily
to a depot, if fewer plants are removed.
6.2
Assumptions, Scope, and Scenarios
Different options were presented in order to fulfil the functional unit and object of the project.
With inspiration from an LCA made by Emilie Berg Hansen at WSP of Case 2, and the internal
document by Plessing (2024), the following key assumptions were made:
• The pipe stretch is 1500 m, where 1000 m is below either gravel path or green areas, and 500
m is below asphalt road.
• In the construction below the asphalt road, it is assumed that asphalt and road section is
removed and disposed off. A new road is established using new gravel and asphalt.
• The width of the pipe trench is 5 m and the depth is 4.6 m.
• Per meter pipe it is assumed that a total of 23 m3 soil is excavated. Of this, 10 m3 soil is
displaced by the pipe and the gravel around the pipe, while 13 m3 is either reintegrated into
the pipe trench or disposed off, depending on the scenario.
• Manholes used in the project are assumed to have a dimension of 1250 mm.
• The combined sewer overflow has a size of 7x7x6 m with a wall thickness of 40 cm and consists
of concrete meaning that each sewer overflow weighs 255.36 tonnes.
50
==PAGE:61==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
• Gravel from the gravel path is assumed to be reused when reestablishing the gravel path. It
is assumed that this is part of the 23 m3 soil excavated in the calculation, and no new gravel
is delivered.
• It is assumed that 0.06 L diesel is used per m2 plants being cleared off.
• The wear and tear on the road from the excavators and transport of the project is assumed
to require 15% reparation on the upper layer. The road is assumed to be 6 m wide and 1500
m long.
The overall system boundary is presented in the process tree in Figure 18. Here, all the processes
included in the calculation are visualised. The production machines and excavators is not included
as it is assumed that they are used for several other projects, and this impact is therefore negligible
relative to this one project. Bracing and the process of installing bracing is also not within the system
boundary. Furthermore, the project expects to use pumps for lowering the level of groundwater on
site (Plessing, 2024). This is not included either.
Figure 18: Process tree of Case 2. The dashed line shows the system boundary, grey boxes are
materials used in the project, brown boxes shows components in the project, green is transport
and installation, and blue is EoL stages. The white boxes are outside the system boundary and
therefore not accounted for.
Based on these assumptions and the scoping, we defined three scenarios in order to compare several
options, and an overview of what is included in each can be seen in Table 10.

6.3
Detailed LCA
As mentioned, an LCA was performed by Emilie Berg Hansen from WSP of Case 2. Her calculations
can be seen in Appendix 11.4.2. She used InfraLCA to perform the analysis, but as in the Detailed
LCA of Case 1, specific processes and EPDs were added manually. It is therefore referred to here
as a Detailed LCA of this case. For each of the three scenarios, assumptions were made for her
calculation:
Scenario 1:
• The concrete pipe has a weight of 5.7 t/m and a dimension of 2500 mm, based on concrete
pipes from IBF (IBF, n.d.a).
• It is assumed that plants are cleared off up to 2.5 m west of the pipe trench, which means that
the road is closed off and less space is available for excavators and soil storage. Therefore, a
depot 2 km from the pipe trench is used for soil storage.
• All soil is moved by a dumper to the depot 2 km away, and afterwards transported 40 km by
truck (32-40 tonnes) to disposal. New soil is transported 40 km to the depot and integrated
from the depot 2 km to the pipe trench.
Scenario 2:
• The concrete pipe is made from more sustainable concrete, Eco2 from IBF, and has a weight
of 5.7 t/m, and a diameter of 2500 mm (IBF, n.d.c). It is calculated using the same emission
factor as in scenario 1, but the production stage (A1-A3) is 50% lower in order to account for
the reduction in CO2-eq from Eco2 concrete.
• It is assumed that plants are cleared off up to 2.5 m west of the pipe trench, which means that
the road is closed off and less space is available for excavators and soil storage. Therefore, a
depot 2 km from the pipe trench is used for soil storage.
• All soil is moved 2 km by a dumper to the depot. Soil that is reintegrated into the pipe trench
is transported back to the pipe trench 2 km by a dumper. The 10 m3 soil that is displaced
per meter pipe is transported 40 km by truck (32-40 tonnes) to disposal after being moved to
the depot. No new soil is needed.
Scenario 3:
• The plastic pipe is based on standard corrugated pipes from InfraLCA of DN 1200.
The
length is doubled to 3000 m instead of 1500 m in order to simulate a plastic pipe of DN 2400.
52
==PAGE:63==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
• It is assumed that plants are cleared off up to 5 m west of the pipe trench, which means that
the road is open to traffic and excavators and has enough space to store soil next to the pipe
trench.
• Soil that is being reintegrated into the pipe trench is placed next to pipe trench, and there
is therefore no transport to the depot. The 10 m3 displaced soil is disposed off directly by a
truck (32-40 tonnes) and transported 40 km.
The full LCI of the detailed calculation for each scenario can be seen in Appendix 11.4.3.
6.4
Screening Tool LCA
The screening tool, described in Section 4, was also used in order compare its performance to the
detailed calculation. The setup of the screening tool, does not allow for selection several types
of previous terrain types. In Case 2, the first 500 m of pipe is below asphalt road, which would
correspond to Paved terrain type in the screening tool.
Meanwhile the final 1000 m of pipe is
below a gravel path or green area, which corresponds to the Unpaved terrain type. Therefore, the
stretch was split into these two parts, called Screening Tool Paved and Screening Tool Unpaved. In
Screening Tool Paved, the road is also re-established, and the Screening Tool Paved was therefore
made in the ’Drainage Project’, while the Screening Tool Unpaved was made in the ’Pipes’ project
type in the screening tool. The full LCI and inputs in the screening tool can be seen in Appendix
11.4.4 and 11.4.5.
In the screening tool, there are no functions for removing or adding plants or repairing a road. These
processes are therefore not calculated in the Screening Tool LCA. It was decided to keep them in
the scope for the calculations in order to investigate the significance of including these processes.
6.5
Results and Discussion
Figure 19 shows the resulting CO2-eq emissions from all three scenarios using both the Detailed-
and the Screening Tool calculation. The result is split into the life cycle stages, A-D (See Figure 1
for an overview of the stages). The results from Screening Tool Paved and Screening Tool Unpaved
are aggregated into a final result.
In Figure 19 it is seen that the impact of the Detailed and the Screening Tool LCA both decrease
from Scenario 1 to 2, but that it decreases in the Detailed LCA in Scenario 3, but increases in
the Screening Tool LCA. The difference is mainly due to the large difference in production (A1-
A3) between the two methods, but also a lack of decreasing impacts in transport (A4) in the
Screening Tool LCA compared to the Detailed LCA. In Scenario 3, a plastic pipe is used, and in
the Detailed LCA, the standard plastic pipe with DN 1200 is used with a length of 3000 m in order
to compensate for the smaller size. In the screening tool, an EPD from Uponor, Weholite (Wang
and Charndramathy, 2022), was used of DN 2500 m. The emission factor is higher for the Weholite
pipe, which therefore also results in higher impacts from A1-A3.
53
==PAGE:64==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Figure 19: Resulting climate change impacts from Case 2 for three scenarios using both the Detailed
LCA and the Screening Tool LCA. The results are split into life cycle stages A-D.
In Figure 19 the use stage, B4, is also added. This captures emissions from replacement of com-
ponents in the system in 50 years. In the Detailed LCA, Stone Mastic Asphalt (SMA) is used in
the road construction as the upper layer. It is denoted as SMA8 (Skærvemastiks) in InfraLCA.
SMA is a dense asphalt mixture with voids filled with asphalt binder, stabilizer and finer aggregate,
which results in a low air void content. Compared to asphalt concrete, SMA makes the pavement
smoother and provides better noise reduction (Ling et al., 2021). SMA is used in combination with
asphalt concrete and ATB (GAB I) in the Detailed calculation. When choosing asphalt concrete
in the screening tool, the combination of materials in the road is based on the catalogue from the
Danish Road Directorate (Vejdirektoratet, 2022), where asphalt concrete is combined with base
course, subbase gravel and ATB, but not with SMA. Asphalt concrete has a lifetime of 50 years
whereas SMA has a lifetime of 16 years in InfraLCA and therefore need replacement, hence the
impact in B4 in the Detailed LCA.
There is also a difference in the impact from stage C and D in the detailed and Screening Tool LCA.
This is mainly due to differences in the emission factor for concrete pipes, concrete manholes, and
concrete sewer overflow between the two calculations.
54
==PAGE:65==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Figure 20: Resulting climate change impacts (sum of A-D) from Case 2 for three scenarios using
both the Detailed LCA and the Screening Tool LCA. The results are split into defined categories.
Figure 20 also shows the resulting emissions from all three scenarios, but here the results are split
into defined categories. The category Road construction includes all processes that have to do with
road construction, including removal of asphalt and gravel in the road section, new gravel, and new
asphalt. The impacts from the road does not change in any of the scenarios, as no optimisations
are done in regard to road construction. The impact from the Detailed LCA is more than double
the impact from the road construction in the screening tool. The main difference is, as explained
earlier, that the screening tool does not included SMA in the road. It might therefore be beneficial
to add the option of including SMA in the screening tool.
The impact from the category Manhole and sewer overflow is 65% smaller in the screening tool than
in the Detailed LCA. This is due to several factors. First of all, the total amount of concrete used for
manholes and sewer overflows in the Detailed LCA is less than 1% smaller than the Screening Tool
LCA, meaning that it is not a difference in the amount, that causes the large difference in impact.
In the Detailed LCA two different emission factors are used for manholes and sewer overflows from
an EPD from Humes (Claudia A. Pe˜na and Rob Rouwette, 2023). In the screening tool, an EPD
from IBF is used for both manholes and sewer overflows (Larsen, Borg, Greibe and Hauan, 2024).
The emission factor from IBF is less than half the emission factor from Humes. The EPD from
Humes has several product groups, where the product group for the sewer overflow is a Culvert,
which is closer to the actual product of a sewer overflow than a manhole. The product group for the
manhole is denoted reinforced concrete pipes. The EPD from IBF is of concrete manholes, where
the concrete is not reinforced. This might be some of the reasons for the large difference in the
emission factor. In term of geographical representativeness, the EPD from Humes covers produc-
tion sites in New Zealand, whereas IBF is located in Denmark. The EPD from IBF is therefore
55
==PAGE:66==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
assessed to have a higher geographical representativeness than Humes for this project, but a lower
technological representativeness in regard to the sewer overflow. Furthermore, in the Detailed LCA
a transport distance of 500 km is assumed for the manholes and sewer overflows, while the screening
tool assumes a transport distance of 107 km. It is difficult to assess which emission factor is closest
to reality as the project has not yet been executed and knowledge of the exact materials used is
therefore not available. But it might be beneficial for the user to change the transport distance of
heavy material such as concrete. The importance of this is investigated further in the sensitivity
analysis in Section 6.6.
The impact from the category Pipe gravel in the screening tool is 4-15 times larger than the impact
in the Detailed LCA. Both methods use the exact same process and emission factor in InfraLCA,
and the difference is therefore only due to differences in amount and transport distance. The amount
used in the screening tool is 1.9-6.9 higher than in the Detailed LCA. Furthermore, the transport
distance of gravel in the Detailed LCA is 50 km, while the screening tool has a transport distance of
132 km. A potential modification to the screening tool could therefore be to be able to change the
amount of gravel needed around the pipe and the transport distance of the gravel. The significance
of this is investigated further in Section 6.6.
The category Soil to and from depot and New soil and soil disposal captures the impacts from soil
transportation on site and soil transportation off site, respectively. The impact from New soil and
soil disposal decreases significantly from Scenario 1 to Scenario 2 using both methods. In Scenario
1, all soil is disposed off, while all the soil, that is not displaced by the pipe and gravel, is reused
in Scenario 2 and 3. Some of this reduction is captured in the screening tool, where it is possible
to choose the amount of reused soil. The impact from Soil to and from depot is reduced more than
80% in Scenario 3, compared to Scenario 1 and 2 in the Detailed LCA. This is due to the lack
of transport of soil, as it is assumed that soil is stored right next to the pipe trench and reused
directly. In the screening tool, the transport distance for reused soil is fixed at 2 km, and the
impact from Soil to and from depot can therefore not be reduced further in Scenario 3. Whether
this is a significant detail to add or not in the screening tool, will be investigated in the sensitivity
analysis in Section 6.6. In Scenario 1 there is no impact from Soil to and from depot in the screening
tool. This is due to the assumption, that if the soil is not reused, it is transported directly to disposal.
A third of the stretch was calculated using the categorisation of Unpaved ground in the screening
tool, corresponding to the 1000 m pipe under green area or gravel path. Here, the excavation profile
is a trapeze, as explained in Section 4.1.2, but it seems to be planned to use bracing along the
entire stretch, meaning that the excavation profile would be rectangle, as in the category Paved
ground in the screening tool. The Screening Tool LCA excavates a total of 1.2 times more soil than
the Detailed LCA, which might be partly due to the assumed trapeze shape of the excavation profile.
It is clear that the largest impact in Case 2 comes from the pipe, as it is a very large pipe. The
results from both the Detailed and Screening Tool LCA agree in terms of the regular concrete pipe
having the highest impact. The impact from the Eco2 concrete pipe and plastic pipe is more am-
biguous. The best way forward would be to gather more data on the actual emissions from both
Eco2 concrete and plastic pipes of this particular size and compare them, as the Detailed LCA
builds upon a smaller plastic pipe with DN 1200, and the screening tool build on a single EPD
(Holder and Udas, 2022), and both use a fixed %-reduction for the calculation of the impact from
Eco2 which does not necessarily applies to such large pipes.
56
==PAGE:67==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Finally the categories Road reparation and Plant removal are only present in the Detailed LCA, as
the screening tool does not offer these options. Plant removal includes both the process of cutting
them down and disposing of the plants. In the Detailed LCA, Road reparation accounts for 1.2-
3.1% of the total impacts of the three scenarios, while Plant removal accounts for 0.3-1.5%. In
this specific case, none of these processes contribute to a large portion of the total impact. Even
so, the impact of a process can be very different depending on the case. If for example, the case
required a very large amount of plants to be removed, this might be an important parameter to
include. But this requires more cases in order to investigate whether they are common parameters
in construction projects within the utility sector.
6.6

Based on the resulting impacts from the three scenarios, a sensitivity analysis was performed for the
categories presented in Figure 20 for both the Detailed and Screening Tool LCA. Table 11 shows
the sensitivity parameter, S, for changing either the amount or the transport distance (in either
A4 or A5, depending on the process) for each of the defined categories. The degree of sensitivity is
colour-coded the same way as in Case 1, according to the classification from Section 3.1.4.
