It comes as no surprise that changing the amount of pipe used in the case is medium to highly sensi-
tive in almost all scenarios. This means that even a small reduction in the pipe size can change the
final impact significantly. Changing the transport distance of the pipe also seems to have somewhat
57
==PAGE:68==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
of an effect, though not as significant as the amount. It is therefore more important that the user
can choose the amount of pipe used, than the transport distance.
Pipe gravel has a medium sensitivity in the Screening Tool LCA in Scenario 1, which makes sense
as this is used to fill up the whole pipe trench. Here it is seen, that it is the transport distance
of the gravel that is important in terms of the impact, as this has a medium sensitivity as well.
Therefore, it might be relevant to be able to change the amount or the transport distance of gravel
in the screening tool.
Changing the amount of manholes and sewer overflows has a minor, but not negligible effect across
all the scenarios and calculation methods. It is therefore beneficial to consider if the amount of
manholes and sewer overflows can be reduced in the project. In relation to this, the transport
distance of manholes and sewer overflows do not seem to be very important, and the transport
distance can therefore be kept predefined in the screening tool.
New soil and soil disposal also has a minor effect in all scenarios and using both the detailed and
screening tool calculation method. This means that benefits can be achieved by reducing the amount
of soil disposed of and the amount of new soil transported to a project site. Most of the emission
from the category New soil and soil disposal, and Soil to and from depot comes from transportation,
and decreasing the transport distance of soil could therefore relevant. The sensitivity of the shorter
distances of Soil to and from depot are negligible according to Case 2.
Changes in the amount of the category Road construction has a minor effect in the Detailed LCA
calculation and a negligible effect in the Screening Tool LCA. This suggests that including the op-
tion of SMA is relevant in the future of the screening tool, as it does impact the resulting emissions
from the road. Transportation of elements in the road construction on the other hand, seems to
be negligible, and this can therefore be predefined in the screening tool without significant conse-
quences to the resulting impacts.
The sensitivity is negligible for Road reparation and Plant removal, which aligns with their small
contribution to the overall impact.
When making these analyses it is tempting to only focus on the large hotspots. For cases with a
very large use phase, it is recommended to make an analysis both with and without them, in order
to identify other hotspots that might be overlooked when there is one high impact (The European
Commission, 2018). When working with a project like Case 2, where there is one large emitter,
the pipe in this case, it could be interesting to omit this from the analysis and see, which other
categories also matter and could be improved. The time has not been available for this analysis in
this report, but this could be a potential feature to investigate, also in terms of the communication
of results in the screening tool.
6.7
Absolute Sustainability
The project of Case 2 has not yet been executed and is in the early planning of the project. There
are therefore only very rough estimations of the construction costs. Based on an estimate retrieved
from the project owner of 110-130 million DKK, an absolute sustainability assessment was made
for each of the three scenarios. The result can be seen in Figure 21.
58
==PAGE:69==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Figure 21: The distribution of the sustainability ratio (SR) of the three scenarios in Case 2. The
range is based on the resulting impact from the Detailed and the Screening Tool LCA. The range
of construction cost is visualised in different colours, with blue corresponding to 110 mil. DKK,
orange to 120 mil. DKK, and green to 130 mil. DKK in total construction costs.
Only Scenario 1 falls above the threshold of the SOS in this assessment, and only when using the
lowest number for the construction costs. Based on this, Scenario 2 and 3 will be classified as
absolute sustainable, when using the allocation methods described in Section 3.2. It should be
noted that it is not clear exactly what the scope of the estimated cost is, and how many processes
are included. Most likely, the costs also include elements like the process of bracing and pumping
up groundwater, which have not been part of the scope in these LCA calculations. If the cost covers
aspects not included in the scope of the LCA of the scenarios, the SR will be underestimated as
a higher investment cost will give a larger share of the SOS to the project in regard of allowed
emissions.
6.8
Conclusion in regard to the Screening Tool
Based on the results from Case 2, potential improvements for the screening tool was found. When
constructing a road, the upper layer called SMA should be added as an option, as this layer consti-
tuted about half of the resulting emissions from road construction in Case 2. The transport distance
of manholes, sewer overflows, and road elements can be kept predefined in the screening tool, as it
is the amount that has the most significant impact on the overall result. The amount of pipe gravel
might be generally overestimated in the screening tool, and this might therefore be a parameter
that can be changed by the user, either through the amount used or the transport distance of it.
The transport distance is the main contributor to emissions from pipe gravel, and by giving the
user the option of changing this, the amount might be kept predefined, as this matters less than
the transport. Soil handling also has an impact, even though relative to other elements in Case 2,
it seemed less significant, and the main impact from soil handling is from its transport. Transport
distance, especially for New soil and soil disposal might be beneficial to be able to change or optimise.
The pipe had the most significant impact on the final result in Case 2, but with large differences in
the emission from the plastic pipes. Further improvement of the screening tool will therefore be to
investigate proper emission factors for large plastic pipes and Eco2 concrete. The transport distance
of pipes could also be added as a parameter, but it is not deemed as important as the amount of pipe
material. It was not clear whether road reparation and plant removal were significant parameters.
Both had a very small impact on the overall result, so it would require looking into other cases with
these elements in order to quantify their importance for construction projects within the utility
sector.
59
==PAGE:70==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
7
Case 3: Fire Station
The third case deals with the site development (Danish: byggemodning) of a new fire- and ambulance
station. The project was designed by WSP, after which it was brought to life by entrepreneurs
(Vejrum, 2023a). The identification of Case 3 is classified, and data that link directly to the case
will therefore not be disclosed in this report. For further information about specifications on the
case, you can contact the authors of this report. The entrepreneurs had the possibility to choose the
products, and were in charge of managing the construction on site. Therefore, much of the needed
data for a detailed LCA is unknown by the consultants at WSP. Due to the lack of precise product
data, it was not possible to do a detailed LCA calculation. The screening tool is tested on the
case, and the results are used to compare the different material amounts with the Bill of Materials
(BOM) created before the start of the Fire station project (Vejrum, 2023b). The BOM was created
as part of the projecting phase (see Section 2.2 for project phases), and is a list of elements included
in the project, their expected amounts and prices.
7.1
System Boundary
The Fire- and Ambulance station consists of many different elements.
The different processes,
within and outside the system boundary, can be found in the process tree in Figure 22.
Figure 22: Process tree for Case 3. The dashed line symbolizes the system boundary. Grey boxes
shows the production of materials, brown boxes shows components in the project, green is transport
and installation, and blue is EoL stages. The white boxes are outside the system boundary and
therefore not accounted for.
The cadastre has different areas for different uses. The main purpose is a station for fire trucks and
ambulances. Therefore, a large part of the outside area is a parking lot with asphalt pavement. The
car shelter, waste sorting, and terrace has concrete tiles as pavement. The rest of the area has green
areas with bushes, trees, and grass fields, and a gravel path going through the area. Underneath
the pavements, a separate sewage system is installed. Some of the rainwater is gathered in the
60
==PAGE:71==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
green space in the corner of the cadastre in a rainwater retention basin, from where the rainwater
runs through drainage pipes into the separate rainwater sewer. The cadastre also has a ditch for
transporting rainwater. The many different functionalities makes it difficult to define an overall
functional unit. An example of a FU could be something similar to: ”a site development to handle
the various needs of an ambulance- and fire station”. The formulation of the FU is rather vague
for this case we will therefore rely on the definition of the system boundary in order to make clear
what is included in the LCA.
7.2
Screening Tool LCA
The calculations in the screening tool are made in the ’Drainage Project’ section in the tool, where
it is only possible to have one pavement type and one pipe length at the same time. Therefore,
three different parts are defined, and calculated separately in the screening tool. The three parts
can be found in Table 12. A full LCI and all inputs to the screening tool can be found in Appendix
11.5.2 and 11.5.3.
Table 12: The division of Case 3 into different parts that are calculated separately in the screening
tool.

7.2.1
Pavements
Some part of the cadastre were already covered in asphalt pavement that had to be removed. Ac-
cording to the BOM, 10 tons of asphalt with a thickness of 12 cm was removed. This corresponds
to 143 m2, when assuming an asphalt density of 70 kg/m2 as InfraLCA for asphalt concrete (AB).
The area of the old asphalt is small compared to what is added in the new project (1350 m2). The
removal of old asphalt is added in part 2, where the size of pavement is 138 m2, even though it
should have been 143 m2, due to the needed size of the new pavement. The removal of the road also
includes the excavation of gravel from beneath the asphalt. The reuse of the gravel is not included
in the screening calculation, although it seems from the BOM that 20 m3 of the gravel is reused
somewhere on the premisses.
In Part 1, it is assumed that the parking lot with asphalt requires a traffic class T2, to allow for
the heavy fire trucks to drive around without damaging the asphalt. This also fits with the BOM,
which specifies that the asphalt layer consists of ATB (GAB I (110 mm)) and asphalt concrete (AB
(3 cm)). The frost susceptibility of the soil is unknown, and the second class, moderately frost
susceptibility, is therefore chosen for all three parts.
61
==PAGE:72==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
The other types of pavements are assumed to be used for walking, and the lowest traffic class (T0)
is therefore chosen in the screening tool. Part 2 of the calculation has 138 m2 of concrete stones as
pavement, which are all assumed to be the same thickness, although they are 7.5 and 10 cm in the
BOM. The last part has the gravel path which is 83 m2 in total. The thickness stated in the BOM
is 10 cm, which is much higher than the 2 cm thickness assumed for road gravel in the screening
tool.
7.2.2
Pipes and Manholes
All pipes in the project are PP pipes. It is not specified whether they are smooth or corrugated.
The pipes for sewage water is DN 160, which in the screening tool only exists as smooth PVC pipes,
which are therefore chosen. For rainwater, the pipes vary between DN 110, 250, and 315. The DN
110 pipes are added in Part 2, whereas the last two (DN 250 and 315) are aggregated into one as
smooth DN 315 plastic pipes in Part 3. The plastic manholes were in sizes DN 315, 425, and 600.
The 10 DN 315 manholes are in Part 1, whereas the last two manhole sizes are added together in
Part 2, since there were only two DN 600 manholes, relative to the ten DN 425 manholes. The
three concrete manholes were DN 1000, 1250, and 1500, and were added together and calculated as
DN 1250 in Part 3.
7.2.3
Ditch and Retention Basin
The BOM includes 250 m2 of Sustainable Urban Drainage System, which includes the rainwater
basin and the ditch. Due to an assessment from the construction drawing, it is assumed that the
ditch is 30 m2, and the basin 220 m2, although this is not specified in the BOM. In the screening
tool, the storage volume for the rainwater basin is assumed to be 220 m3, since the depth is not
specified in the BOM. The ditch is assumed to be 30 m long, 0.5 m deep, and 1 m wide, but none
of this is specified in the BOM either. In both the ditch and the basin, stones are added to keep
the soil in the right place. Stones are not possible to add in the screening tool. The BOM does
not mention any use of membranes in either of the two. The current version of the screening tool
includes a membrane in the rainwater basin, which is not possible for the user to remove. The ditch
is calculated without a membrane, so it fits with the BOM. According to the BOM, the basin has a
drain grate which lets the water down to the drains. This fits well with the drains that are included
in the dry rainwater basin in the screening tool.
7.2.4
Soil Handling
It is unspecified in the BOM, how much of the soil from the pipe trench that can be reused as
filling. Part of the surplus soil will be disposed. Since the actual reuse percentage is unknown, it is
assumed to be 50%. When doing the LCA calculation in the screening tool, all of the surplus soil
is disposed to a soil handling site, even though some of the soil might be locally reintegrated.
7.3
Results and Discussion
The total impact from the Fire station was 58,214 kg CO2-eq.
The total impacts on Climate
Change (CO2-eq) can be found in Figure 23, split into defined categories. Calculations can be
found in Appendix 11.5.1.
62
==PAGE:73==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
Figure 23: Results for Case 3 from the calculations in the screening tool. The graph shows categories
of processes divided into the modules A-D.
Figure 23 shows that the highest impact comes from the gravel used beneath the roads in the project
(base course and subbase gravel). The main part of the emissions in this category stems from the
transport stage (A4), which fits well with the tendency seen for gravel materials in Case 1 and 2,
due to its density and relatively large transport distance (132 km) assumed in the screening tool.
The pavement category, which contains asphalt, concrete tiles, and the gravel for the path, has the
second largest impact. The majority of impacts from pavements comes from the two asphalt layers
(ATB 1 and asphalt concrete). The concrete paving stones are supposed to cover 138 m2. In the
screening tool, a 5 cm stone is assumed to be strong enough for a T0, whereas the actual stones used
are 7.5 and 10 cm. The emissions from the screening tool in regard to the concrete paving stones
are therefore underestimated. The same tendency is seen for the path gravel. In the screening tool,
it is assumed that 2 cm of path gravel is enough for T0, whereas the BOM requires a layer of 10 cm
for the 83 m2. The screening tool therefore also underestimates the impact from the gravel path
with a factor 5, due to different layer thickness expectations.
Emissions from pipes and manholes has the fifth largest impact (Figure 23). The emissions primar-
ily stems from the production stage (A1-A3), which is relatively low since the majority of drainage
elements were plastic with small dimensions. The impact from demolition of old asphalt is small, as
well as the impact from the membranes and flow regulator used in the basin. The details about the
ditch and rain water basin were limited, why the results from these come with some uncertainties.
They primarily have an impact in regard to soil disposal, which is 7.5 m3 for the ditch and 250
m3 for the retention basin. The impacts from the ditch are small, since no membrane is used, and
the volume of soil for disposal is limited. Both the ditch and the retention constructed on site had
63
==PAGE:74==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
stones for avoiding erosion of the soil on the sides, which are not captured in the screening tool
calculations. The impact from the ditch is therefore underestimated. On the other hand, the use
of a plastic membrane in the basin in the screening tool does not match the actual basin, and its
impacts might therefore be overestimated. For future versions of the screening tool, it could be
considered to add more details in the ditch and basin categories, to heighten the quality of the
calculations in the screening phase.
A selection of results for material amounts were compared to the Bill of Materials (BOM). The
comparison can be found in Figure 24.
Figure 24: Comparison of amounts (m3) calculated in the screening tool (blue) and the specified
amounts in the BOM (orange).
The amounts of base course, used beneath pavements, are similar, as seen in Figure 24. Despite
the base course fitting well, there is a large mismatch in the amount of subbase gravel calculated
in the screening tool, compared to the amount assumed in the BOM. The screening tool suggests
a 15 cm layer of base course beneath T2 asphalt (see Section 4.1.1), which fits well with the 16
cm that is stated in the BOM. On the other hand, the screening tool assumes a 20 cm layer of
subbase gravel, whereas the BOM specifies a 55 cm layer. In the calculation, a moderately frost
susceptibility is assumed, together with a traffic class 2 (T2) for the asphalt parking lot, and T0 for
the paved terrace and path. The significance of changing the traffic class and frost susceptibility is
further investigated in Section 7.4.
The pipe trench gravel has the third largest contribution to the impacts from the project (Figure
23), which again, primarily stems from the A4 stage. A minimum of 20% pipe trench gravel in
the trench is assumed in the screening tool, and in this case, it is assumed that only 50% of the
excavated soil is reused as filling in the pipe trench, which means that extra gravel needs to be
64
==PAGE:75==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
added in the trench. The amount of pipe trench filling calculated by the screening tool is 38%
larger than the amount specified in the BOM, which suggests that the screening tool overestimates
the need gravel need in the pipe trench. The needed amount of pipe trench filling in the screening
tool is directly linked to the size of the pipe trench and the amount of reused soil. Therefore, if the
soil disposal is high, more gravel needs to be added to fill the pipe trench again. Soil for disposal is
the fourth largest contribution to emissions in the project, since it is assumed that all surplus soil is
disposed. In practice, more soil might have been reintegrated on site, as suggested by the amount of
soil for disposal being higher in the screening tool than in the BOM in Figure 24. The soil disposal
from pipes in the screening tool is therefore also overestimated compared to the BOM. This could
be explained by one or more of the following three things. Firstly, it was seen in Case 1 that the
screening tool is conservative in its amount of soil excavated from the pipe trench. Secondly, it was
chosen to use the 1-2 m pipe depth, since this depth best covered the majority of the pipes, although
some pipes were placed in less that 1 meter depth. This means that more soil was removed than
neccesary in the screening tool. Lastly, it should be noted that the reuse percentage of 50% was
assumed, since there was no knowledge on the topic. The significance of decreasing the amount of
soil excavated from the trench and thereby lowering the gravel amount, or increasing the degree of
soil reuse, is examined in the sensitivity analysis in Section 7.4. For future work the effect of adding
other types of filling, e.g. new soil, to the pipe trench instead of gravel could be investigated.
7.4
Sensitivity Analysis
Different aspects of the case were tested for their sensitivity. The sensitivity coefficient is found by
using the formula described in Section 3.1.4, and the results can be found in Table 13.
Table 13: Sensitivity coefficients for elements in Case 3. Green: S>0.05, Yellow: S>0.3, and Red:
S>0.5.
Change from baseline
S
Gravel and concrete pavement to higest traffic class
0.08
All pavements to higest traffic class
0.62
Gravel distances
0.47
Transport of soil to disposal
0.10
Transport of soil to and from depot
0.01
Soil degree of reuse
0.44
The sensitivity of the traffic class is tested by changing the concrete pavement stones from T0 to T2
and the gravel path from T0 to T1. It is investigated since the concrete stones thickness for T2 in
the screening tool is 8 cm, compared to 5 cm in T0. It therefore fits better with the wanted 7.5 cm
in the BOM. The gravel layer is 2 cm in the screening tool, compared to 10 cm in the BOM. When
the gravel pavement is changed from T0 to T1 in the screening tool, the layer of pavement does not
change, but the thickness of the base course and subbase gravel increases. When the traffic classes
for the concrete pavement and the gravel path is changed, the overall impact increases 4%. Part
of the increase in impact comes from the extra gravel in the road section where the used amounts
increase by 5% for the subbase gravel, and 6% for the base course. As seen in Table 13, changing
the traffic class for the two pavements have low sensitivity (0.08).
When changing all the three pavements to the highest possible traffic classes in the screening tool,
T3 for asphalt, T2 for concrete paving stones, and T1 for the gravel path, the overall impact in-
65
==PAGE:76==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
creased with 22%. The sensitivity of changing all pavements is high (S=0.62) in this project, which
suggests that the asphalt layer is highly significant, compared to when only changing the concrete
pavement stones and gravel path. This means that it is significant for the user to consider lower
traffic classes, especially for larger areas. The needed amount of subcase gravel rose to 437 m3,
which is slightly closer to the amount of 905 m3 specified in the BOM compared to the previously
calculated amount, see Figure 24. When also changing the frost susceptibility from moderate to
high, there is no change in gravel use, since it is assumed that the same thickness of subcase gravel
is needed, as seen in Table 1 in Section 4.1.1. On the other hand, if the ground had been frost
resistant, no subcase gravel would have been required.
Transport of gravel materials results in high emissions, and the influence of changing the transport
distance was therefore investigated. The gravel distances are medium sensitive, but close to highly
sensitive (0.47) (as seen in Table 7.4), which fits well with the tendency seen in Case 1 and 2.
The degree of soil for reuse in the pipe trench was tested for decreasing the soil amount, and
thereby the gravel amount. The sensitivity coefficient is 0.44, and the degree of reuse thereby has
significant influence on the result and should be taken into account. Another approach for lowering
soil amounts for disposal and gravel in the pipe trench, would be if the user could change the size
of the pipe trench. This is currently not possible, but the feature could be considered for future
work. Lastly, the transport of soil to disposal or depot was also investigated for its sensitivity. It
was found that the transport distance of soil to disposal is more sensitive than the transport of soil
to depot, but that it is minor sensitive compared to the soil reuse degree.

The cost has a high impact on the SR result. In this assessment, it is therefore a problem that
some elements from the BOM are included in the system boundary, and others are excluded, since
it makes it more difficult to asses the price of the project analysed within the system boundary.
Due to uncertainties of which prices to include in the scope, other prices were also considered. 1.7
mil. DKK represents the low price, and 2.1 mil. DKK is the price where all elements in doubt
are considered. The total climate impact calculated in the screening tool is 58214.14 kg CO2-eq,
which corresponds to a sustainability ratio, SR, of 0.93. The SR for the case is therefore classified
as absolute sustainable according to this method. For the lower price the SR is 1.04, which means
that the project is no longer considered to be below the planetary boundary.

higher than what is calculated in the results (baseline), due to higher thicknesses in the road section
66
==PAGE:77==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
and pavements than what is assumed by the screening tool. This scenario with a larger impact (74.5
t CO2-eq) is therefore also assessed as a possible emission from the project, leading to a resulting
SR, for all three prices, above the planetary boundary. This clearly shows the effect of different
price ranges and uncertainties of emissions on the assessment of absolute sustainability.
7.6
Conclusion in regard to the Screening Tool
Firstly, the results gave insight to some possible limitations in the way the screening tool calculates
the material amounts. The pavements seemed to be underestimated, and soil disposal and gravel for
pipe trench filling seems overestimated (see Figure 24). Both the amount of pavement and subbase
gravel are lower in the results from the screening tool than what is expected in the BOM. This
suggests that the assumed amounts based on traffic classes, based on the uses of the paved areas,
are underestimated in this case. The sensitivity analysis showed that changing the traffic classes of
all pavements have a high impact on the overall result, and this should therefore be considered in
cases like this one, where a large part of the area is covered in pavement.
In the project report it is mentioned that the entrepreneurs can reuse soil if it is suitable (Vejrum,
2023a). The actual resulting proportion of reused soil from the site is unknown, so it could be
more or less than what is assumed in the calculations. Soil reuse has an impact on the result, since
transportation of soil from and to the site has a high impact. As in the results for Case 1 and
2, the screening tool seems conservative in the calculation of gravel for pipe trench filling. The
investigations in this case suggests that the screening tool is also conservative in regards to soil
excavation. This could be solved by making it possible for the user to decrease the amount of soil
excavated in future versions of the screening tool.
The amount of gravel in this project is high, especially in the road section, which emphasises the
need for a focus on resource use in the screening tool. Based on the sensitivity analysis, it can be
concluded that degree of soil for reuse, and the distance to the gravel pit, have great importance
for the resulting emissions from Case 2, since they have a medium, almost highly sensitive, with S
being 0.48 and 0.47 respectively. The distance from gravel pits and degree of reused soil, should
therefore be considered when planning the project.
The screening tool is able to calculate many of the elements in Case 2 in Part 1, 2, and 3, but several
things outside the system boundary had to be excluded. For example vegetation, curbstones, drains,
electric installation, etc., are not included in the screening tool. These are therefore not part of
the scope of the calculation, even though all elements on site are connected with each other. This
multi-dependency of different objects on each other is difficult to capture in the screening tool.
67
==PAGE:78==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
8
Summarising Discussion
The specific results from each of the cases were discussed within each of their sections and sum-
marised in Sections 5.11, 6.8, and 7.6, respectively. This section therefore seeks to discuss the main
takeaways from the results from the cases, the overall uncertainties and assumptions in regard to
the inputs, calculations, and outputs of the screening tool, and its potential use and implementation
in the utility sector.
The results from screening tool has been tested in Case 1, 2, and 3, but it was also sought to
get feedback on the overall structure and use of the screening tool.
Some of the interviewees
were already introduced in the interviews in Section 2.3, with Mads Lenschau and a Jon Kjær
Jørgensen from the Danish Road Directorate, Bettina Precht Simonsen from the utility company
KLAR Forsyning, and Jacob Alexander Damkjær from the utility company VandCenter Syd all
being helpful in trying out the screening tool and providing feedback (Simonsen, 2025; Damkjær,
2025; Lenschau and Jørgensen, 2024). Several meetings were also held at WSP. The first was a
meeting with the sewage specialist Jesper Madsen, who has great knowledge about the technical-
and practical aspects of the construction of pipe stretches, manholes, and the road section and
pavements often added on top. He also assisted with assumptions in regard to the calculations
for the pipe trenches in the screening tool (Madsen, 2024), explained in Section 4.1.2. A group
meeting with project leaders from WSP Denmark was also held.
They have a lot of technical
knowledge, with a focus on the landscape aspects (retention basins and ditches), and on pipes and
roads (WSP Group meeting, 2025). Lastly, from WSP, LCA specialist, Emilie Berg Hansen, also
provided her feedback. Her knowledge about LCA meant that she had another focus when giving
feedback (Hansen, 2025). The feedback is presented in relevant sections in the following discussion
of the aspects of the screening tool.
8.1
The Cases
The cases represent different types of construction projects within the utility sector, but also have
their limits. Case 1 tests its ability to calculate a separate sewer system on unpaved grounds, while
Case 2 is a combination of a combined sewer system and paved and unpaved ground, with the
construction of a road as well. Case 3 combines all components in the screening tool, but only pro-
vides a single ditch and retention basin to analyse. The cases therefore give a good overview of the
performance of the screening tool in regard to pipe installation and asphalt roads, while other types
of pavements are less investigated as well as ditches and retention basins. Testing the performance
of the screening tool on more cases with these elements could therefore be a future improvement of
the tool.
Only Case 1 presents data from the actual amount of soil excavated, specific pipes and manholes
used, amount of gravel, and energy consumption from machines and excavators. The results from
the screening tool can therefore be compared to ’true’ values. Meanwhile, Case 2 is a project that
is still in the early planning phase, and the detailed calculation is therefore based on assumptions
by employees at WSP. Case 3 is a finished project, but data on the final consumption of material
was not available, and therefore the analysis was based on the Bill of Materials. It is therefore only
Case 1, that can be used to assess the performance of the screening tool on the actual impact, while
Case 2 and 3 gives an indication of how well the screening tool aligns with data and assumptions
based on technicians estimates and their experience.
68
==PAGE:79==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
8.2
Roads
In the group meeting at WSP, the lack of being able to reuse the road section was noted, as this is
often a consideration when planning. This should therefore be an option in the tool or at least be
investigated, how this would affect resulting impacts. The term ”Paved” vs. ”Unpaved”, needed
to be specified, as the testers thought that it determined the final state of the project, not the
conditions before project start, which is the actual meaning in the screening tool (WSP Group
meeting, 2025). When users select a paved surface before project start, the tool currently assumes
it is an asphalt road which needs to be demolished. It might as well be other types of pavement
in reality. Demolition of the old asphalt layer had little impact in both Case 2 and 3, but impacts
from removing other types of pavements have not been investigated. The significance of demolition
of different types of pavements is therefore unknown. This could be a further investigation in order
to asses if users should have the option to specify the type of existing pavement - and choose if
parts of it should be reused. Furthermore, if the user wants to combine several types of either
existing or new pavement in the road construction, the project has to be split up in order to make
the calculation in the screening tool. This is inconvenient.
In Case 3, it was found that the amount of base course calculated by the screening tool fitted well
with the amount specified in the BOM, whereas the subbase gravel was underestimated by the
tool. This suggests that even though the thickness follow standards from catalogues, they might
not always correspond to the actual values used. It was also found that the sensitivity of changing
traffic classes is high. In similar projects, where a large part of the area is covered with pavement,
it is therefore important that the user have the option to choose between different traffic classes.
As amounts are tied to traffic classes, it should be made transparent to user, how their results are
affected by the traffic class.
8.3
Pipes and Concrete Constructions
Generally, it was found to be important that the user can choose the amount of pipe material,
manholes, and sewer overflow, as these contribute significantly to the resulting impacts in Case 1
and 2. But at the group meeting at WSP, it was noted that the length of the pipes in the separate
sewage system should be differentiated. Often the sewage water would go to the wastewater treat-
ment plant, whereas the rainwater would end in e.g. a rainwater basin and therefore have different
lengths (WSP Group meeting, 2025).
The sensitivity from transport of plastic pipes in Case 1 was found to be negligible, despite the
long assumed transport distance (1500 km). This might be due to the relatively low density (18
kg/m pipe for DN 600 corrugated pipe in InfraLCA). A study found that emissions from a truck
carrying a high-density material is 0.140 kg CO2-eq per tkm, whereas it is 0.242 kg CO2-eq per
tkm for a low-density material truck (Soust-Verdaguer et al., 2022). Since InfraLCA only considers
the weight of the products, there is a risk that the emissions from transport of e.g. plastic pipes
is underestimated, as InfraLCA might assume the truck to be more ’full’ than what is realistic.
This could be the case with the large pipes in Case 2, where it was mentioned that maximum of
two pipes can be transported per truck. The modelling of the impacts from transport in InfraLCA
could be considered in future work.
Generally, the main focus in the report has been on assessing the amounts in regard to input, while
emission factors have less focus. But emission factors also affect the result, as seen in Case 1 with
Ultra Rib 2 Blue, a more sustainable plastic pipe with significantly lower emission factor compared
69
==PAGE:80==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
to the rest, in Case 2 with the large differences in resulting impacts from the large plastic pipes in
Scenario 3 between the Detailed and the Screening Tool LCA, and for the concrete sewage overflows
and manholes in Case 2, with EPDs from Humes, New Zealand, compared to IBF in Denmark. This
exemplifies the importance of assuring an emission factor with high representativeness, as the final
result can strongly depend on it. The investigation of the distribution of concrete pipes in Section
5.8.1 shows how the emission factor of large concrete pipes vary more than the smaller concrete
pipes. This suggests that emission factors from large components can give large variations in the
resulting impacts if used in large quantities. In contrast, the investigation of the distribution of
gravel did not show large variations among different gravel types, thereby suggesting that simplifying
the analysis by using one type of gravel, will not change the final impact significantly. The best
way forward is by collecting more data points to create averages in order to capture the highest
representativeness of the products used and avoid errors caused by single EPDs.
8.4
Retention Basins
The calculations of the retention basins are based on specific previous projects and changes by the
user are limited. This means that we make most of the assumptions on behalf of the user, and
if some of those assumptions are imprecise or incorrect, it leads to unavoidable errors in the final
result. At the group meeting at WSP, the definition of the of storage volume was found confusing
in e.g. the wet basin, where part of the volume is constantly wet. They suggested to phrase the
term as ”excavated volume”, because the excavated soil volume is often larger than the final storage
volume, since the landscape often needs to be adjusted. ”Excavated volume” would thereby include
both storage volume and wet volume (WSP Group meeting, 2025). For further improvements of the
screening tool, it would be beneficial to look further into the storage calculations, and spend time
formulating the needs of each storage type, as is done for the other categories in the screening tool.
More of the details could be chosen by the user, e.g. soil handling (disposal vs. local reintegration),
wet-volume size, recycled plastic and so on. This would add an extra level of user flexibility to the
screening tool.
8.5
Soil and Gravel
Based on all three cases, amount- and transport of gravel contributed significantly to the overall
emissions, and for soil, transportation was the main parameter affecting its emissions. But amounts
of both soil and gravel also seemed to be overestimated in all three cases. The calculation of overlap
in separate sewer systems, explained in Section 4.1.2, had several uncertainties. The overlap is
calculated based on pipes in different depths, while the screening tool always assumes pipes to be
in the same depth. The distance between the pipes is kept constant at 1.5 m, which in reality is
not always the case and should ideally also change with pipe size. The size of the pipe trench is
predefined in the screening tool, which makes the calculation less flexible. This was seen in Case
1, where the screening tool was unable to capture the optimised soil amounts in Scenario 2 and
3. The assumptions that have been made are conservative, in order to best capture the ”baseline”
scenario, but in practice, less soil might be removed than what is calculated in the screening tool.
An optimization could therefore be to allow the user to excavate less soil. Additionally, the user
could be able to choose the amount of minimum pipe trench filling, which is currently 20% of the
excavated soil.
Furthermore, the tool should offer more options for using alternative materials for pipe trench fill-
ing, such as crushed materials (concrete, tiles, and asphalt) or lime-stabilized soil, which was also
proposed by Jesper (Madsen, 2024). Julie Katrine Jensen, senior project leader at the soil handling
70
==PAGE:81==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
company Norrecco, told how they have started ”washing” surplus soil from projects to create an
alternative to virgin raw materials. Soil contains many different sizes of particles, which are sorted
by washing with water. It is sorted into pebble, gravel, and sand. The remaining fraction are the
finest particles clay and silt, where contaminants also ends up being stored. This small fraction is
disposed off, whereas the pebble, gravel, and sand can be reused (Jensen, 2024a). Julie argues that
a soil-waste hierarchy should be considered to ensure more direct reuse within the construction sec-
tor. Firstly, one should consider options for direct reuse on-site. Secondly, the soil could be treated
at a soil facility, or on-site, with lime stabilisation or soil washing. Then, as a last solution, the soil
can be put to use in projects like Lynetteholmen, where slightly contaminated soil is used for city
expansion (Jensen, 2024a). This emphasises the optimisation options of local reintegration of soil
on-site, but also gives new possibilities for disposed soil, which, if handled correctly, can be reused
in other projects. The emissions from a mixture of crushed concrete and tiles is 2.99 kg CO2-eq per
tonne and for a mix of concrete and asphalt is 2.41 kg CO2-eq per tonne in the production stage
(A1-A3) (RGS Nordic, 2024a), which is similar to the impacts of virgin gravel materials. Even
though reductions in emissions are less prevalent, raw material extraction in Denmark is worth
considering, since gravel is a limited resource, as reflected upon in Section 4.4.2. In the future this
will lead to longer travel distances and higher prices, since the demand is projected to rise (Danske
Regioner, 2020; Dagens Byggeri, 2024). These alternatives for gravel materials should therefore be
considered in order to decrease the gravel extraction. Lime-stabilised soil has an impact of 14.2
kg CO2-eq per tonne in the production stage (A1-A3) due to the high emissions from lime (RGS
Nordic, 2024b). Therefore, lime-stabilised soil should only be used to conserve virgin raw materials.
The terms ”degree of reusability” of the soil and the ”disposal/local reintegration” can be difficult
to differentiate, and all the interviewees found the formulation unclear. A more explicit explanation
was therefore added in the tool. Jesper argued that it would be beneficial for the user to be able
choose their own distances for disposal- and local reintegration. This could be added in future
versions of the tool, while still keeping a default value for the users, who are in doubt about the
transport distances in the early planning stages (Madsen, 2024). As it is meant as a screening
tool, the input parameters should be available in the early phase of project planning. This means
that a balance between easily available input parameters and reasonable assumptions of all other
parameters needs to be assessed. This was investigated in the sensitivity analyses of the cases,
where transport of large amounts of gravel was found to have a medium sensitivity in all three
cases. Bettina mentioned that it is rarely known from where gravel is retrieved early in the project
planning (Simonsen, 2025). So, even though this parameter is impactful on the final result, it might
not be a pragmatic parameter to add, if proper inputs cannot be given. It should also be noted,
that gravel and soil in general seemed to overestimated in the screening tool, so this should also be
looked into. The benefit of adding the option of decreasing transport either way, would be for the
user to become aware of the importance of how transport of heavy materials affect their outputs,
in line with the objective of the screening tool to increase knowledge of environmental impacts of
different scenarios of construction projects. This could also be done by adding an optimisation
option with predefined amounts of decreased transport or amount of soil or gravel, thereby not
requiring specific inputs from the user.
In the Danish newspaper Ingeniøren, 10th of January 2025, the CO2-emissions related to the large
Danish ”Infrastructure agreement 2035” from 2021 were examined (Andersen, 2025). The agree-
ment includes 160 billion Danish kroner investments in infrastructure until 2035.
The Danish
Road Directorate and the Danish Railway Authority (Banedanmark) are in charge of planning the
projects. They used InfraLCA to do the final calculations that gave a result 121% higher than the
71
==PAGE:82==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
calculations made prior to the agreement in 2021 using an older and unspecified tool. The Danish
Road Directorate explains that the old tool underestimated several elements, whereas InfraLCA is
more detailed and precise in its calculations. The emissions from transport of materials to and from
the construction site had proven to be up to four times larger than first assumed. The increase in
emissions worried politicians, and has led to considerations of cancelling or postponing the projects
(Andersen, 2025). This case proves the importance of not underestimating the initial calculations.
From this, it is concluded that it is preferable for the screening tool to be overestimating rather
than underestimating impacts of projects in the early planning phases.
8.6
Optimisations
Optimisation options are key to helping the users spot the places where they can make a difference.
It was therefore important to assess different options for optimisation throughout the programming
of the tool. Some of the optimisations are explained in a userform when making a new scenario, in
order to prompt sustainable thinking and give suggestions to how the user can change their inputs
to optimise the project, e.g. by a lower pipe diameter or traffic class. Other optimisations are pre-
defined, for example more sustainable concrete, which automatically applies to all concrete pipes, if
chosen. It is important that the user is aware about uncertainties in regard to the impact of more
sustainable new materials. The CO2-eq saved in the production stage could easily be replaced with
an increase in CO2-eq from the transport stage, if the optimised materials are produced further
away. This issue is not currently accounted for in the screening tool and is important to be aware
of in order not to compromise savings of emissions through apparent optimisation.
Bettina suggested to add the idle run of machinery as an optimization. She pointed out that the
standard amount of idling corresponds to 40% of the total time the machines are running. If the user
could decrease the idling time spend to a lower percentage, it could be an interesting optimisation
option, especially for the emissions from A5 (Simonsen, 2025). This optimization is difficult to add
in the screening tool, as it would require knowledge about the diesel use in idling vs. when doing
heavy work. It was therefore chosen not to implement it in the screening tool for now. We have
been made aware of some optimisations through the case studies, e.g. Ultra Rib 2 blue plastic and
Eco2 concrete pipes, and recycled gravel. The interest for sustainability is rising, and we therefore
expect that several new products and materials will enter the market. If wanting to account for
newer, more sustainable options, the screening tool should include updates of new products.
Plants, especially trees, acts as a natural carbon sink, which could help decrease the CO2-emissions
over the lifetime of a project. As mentioned in Section 2.1.3, biodiversity or biosphere integrity,
and climate change area core planetary boundaries. Including plants in the screening tool would
give the user the opportunity to optimize their project in regards to biodiversity as well as climate
change. Studies find varied results when trying to asses the effect of trees for carbon sequestration.
A Canadian study found that planting trees around buildings could help decrease the overall impact
between 3-17% over a 60-year period (Grossi et al., 2023), whereas another study, from Helsinki,
found that the CO2 uptake from urban trees is balanced out by respiration from both the tree itself
and the soil surrounding the roots, and the effect of trees as carbon sinks in cities therefore is very
uncertain (Havu et al., 2022). If the user in the future is able to include trees in the screening
calculation, it is important to consider the uncertainties of the CO2 uptake.
It should also be
mentioned that quantifying biodiversity impacts is challenging, and not necessarily ties easily to
the LCA method (Damiani et al., 2023). Biodiversity has therefore not been part of the scope of
this screening tool.
72
==PAGE:83==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
8.7
Aesthetics, User-friendliness, and Communication of Results
Overall, the feedback received on the visual aspects of the tool was positive, Jacob was fond of the
layout (Damkjær, 2025), and Bettina mentioned that she found the tool intuitive to use (Simonsen,
2025). Mads and Jon valued the implementation of the different categories (e.g. road or sewage
systems) with LCA, the ease of use, and the opportunity to perform a quick LCA (Lenschau and
Jørgensen, 2024).
Regarding the result page in the screening tool, Emilie mentioned that the graphs give a good
overview, but that the absolute sustainability thermometer needs more explanation on the page for
people unaware of the definitions (Hansen, 2025). Jacob was positive in regard to the inclusion of
absolute sustainability, which they have also worked with in their company (Ryberg et al., 2019),
although he agrees that the actual calculation is complex, making the estimate uncertain (Damkjær,
2025). Mads and Jon found absolute sustainability relevant to include, and they were looking for-
ward to see the final version (Lenschau and Jørgensen, 2024). At the group meeting at WSP, they
found some of the terms difficult to understand, especially the stages A-D (WSP Group meeting,
2025). It is therefore recommended to add explanations of these. In future versions, A5 could also
de divided into installation and transport, which would give a better overview, especially in the soil
handling category. They also mentioned that the graphs were difficult to read, as some categories or
processes are much higher than others and can overshadow other categories (WSP Group meeting,
2025). This could be solved by allowing to omit some categories in the visualisation, if these are
dominating the picture.
Jacob was positive about the figure that shows the gravel use, since they often work with decreasing
the amount of gravel used in their projects. He explained that the prices of gravel are expected
to rise in the coming years due to the depletion of Danish gravel pits. A decrease in gravel use
is therefore not only better in regard to climate change, but also for the economy of the project
(Damkjær, 2025).
Jesper commented that the formulations and terms needed to be clearer in the screening tool
(Madsen, 2024). The design of the screening tool was made with non-LCA experts in mind. The
formulations are therefore essential in order to make results of the screening tool comparable across
different stakeholders. Difficulties in understanding formulations in the screening tool was therefore
prioritised through the addition of help buttons in the userforms and an introduction in the tool.
As an add-on, a brief overview of the assumptions behind the calculations ’underneath’ the LCA,
could be made available to the users, in order to make the calculations transparent. A draft of this
was made and can be seen in Appendix 11.1.2.
It would be beneficial if the results can be aggregated and ”printed” in a separate Word- or pdf-file,
which the user can save and use for the further planning process. It should include graphs, tables
and explanations, together with the input that the user chose for the different scenarios. This could
be added for later versions.
8.8
General Limitations of the Screening Tool
The Excel-file with the screening tool has grown to include many different sheets, data, and large
amounts of coding in the VBA-programming. There is a risk that mistakes have been made along
the way, but a quality check was made through the use of the tool on the cases in order to minimise
potential human errors. Due to the large Excel-file, the speed of calculation is decreased, as also
73
==PAGE:84==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
commented by Jacob (Damkjær, 2025). Mads and Jon mentioned that the computation capacity in
a large Excel-file is a limitation, that they also face when using the standard version of InfraLCA.
They therefore encouraged our choice of keeping the calculations of the categories separate from the
emission calculations to make sure assumptions and calculations can be transferred to other types
of programmes in the future and not just be tied to InfraLCA in Excel (Lenschau and Jørgensen,
2024). In line of this they added that the calculations and assumptions going into each process are
more important than the specific emission factors. The inputs are time-consuming to define and
potential errors in emission factors will apply to all projects and are easier to correct, unlike specific
calculations of inputs (Lenschau and Jørgensen, 2024).
The data used in the screening tool comes with uncertainties, but as most data originates from
EPDs, the uncertainties have not been quantified. The data quality of emission factors was gener-
ally assessed to be high, but this could be a further investigation in the future of how well EPDs
actually performs as input in other LCA studies. The distribution of data was examined in Case
1, section 5.8, and generally the data points did not deviate significantly, or at least did not give
reasons for being omitted from the averages.
The tool is meant for screening, and therefore only gives a first rough estimation. Accuracy is
important, but total accuracy is by the nature of screening unattainable, as the data required
for full accuracy is not available at the time of screening. Optimally, the process of developing a
screening tool should be iterative. When a project is finished, the result of the actual material
use and energy consumption should be compared to the estimate from the screening tool in the
beginning of the planning phase. This creates a circle of adjusting the calculations of the screening
tool with real life data constantly, and in this way the accuracy of the screening tool should be
increased with time.
8.9
Absolute Sustainability
The choice of adding the concept of absolute sustainability arose from the wish of being able to
communicate some kind of threshold to stay within in order to be ’truly’ sustainable. In this report,
the method was used on Case 2 and 3, where the sustainability ratio ranged between 0.3-1.4. These
construction projects are therefore deemed as absolute sustainable or just above the threshold of
absolute sustainability, using these allocation methods. Case 1 displayed the relation between the
SR of KLAR Forsyning and the life time of their projects, which showed that after about 30 years,
their activities seem absolute sustainable. This is good news in terms of the planet, but the purpose
of adding an absolute sustainability assessment in the screening tool was also to give an incentive
to get below the threshold. If construction projects in the utility sector in general fall below this
threshold, it might lead to thinking that what we do now is good enough, leading to lack of action.
Since there are many uncertainties tied to the calculation, further investigation could be put into
assessing how accurate the results are.
The first allocation method used in this report assumes that the investment cost can be used as
proxy for consumption preferences. This reflection makes sense, as we often spend money on that
which we prioritise and find valuable. But there could be situations where our willingness to pay for
something is not reflected in the actual costs of something. It is therefore deemed as a pragmatic
proxy in this report, but optimally it should be combined with an assessment of the needs and wants
of the consumers. The second allocation method builds on the principle of grandfathering, which,
in terms of climate justice, is deemed less ethical. But due to lack of data and difficulties assessing
74
==PAGE:85==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
variables such as needs, responsibilities, or capacities, this was the most accessible principle to use.
Both allocation methods used use the investment costs of the construction project as main input.
On the positive side, it is an accessible data input, also in the early planning process. The other
allocation method considered, were based on the number of people serviced through the project.
This can be quite ambiguous with several dependencies and is not easily accessible as a user input.
But the negative side of only using investment costs as an input is that the easy way of making a
project seem sustainable would be to raise the investment costs. Luckily, economy is often a (very)
important parameter in decision-making, and the main objective is usually to cost-optimise. The
incentive to raise the costs of a project is therefore assessed to be low.
Allocating based on investment costs seem rather straightforward, but it is important to keep the
scope in mind. It should only be the construction costs tied to the exact elements and their depen-
dencies that are used as input in order not to increase the scope to more, or less, than what is being
calculated in the screening tool. E.g. for Case 2 and 3, it has not been clear how many processes
were included in the construction costs, as it they were rough estimates. There is therefore a risk
of underestimating SR of a project, if too much is included in the total costs, that is not included
in the LCA performed by the screening tool. This can be partly avoided by using a range, if there
is doubt about the investment cost for the specific analysis.
The SOS for GHG emission is given as CO2-eq/year, meaning that is has a time dimension. A nor-
mal LCA does not have a time dimension. In this report, this discrepancy is solved by dividing the
total impact with the assumed lifetime of the project. Even though basically all impacts only hap-
pen during the construction of the project, the emissions are artificially divided evenly throughout
its total lifetime. Other studies have used the approach of making a consumption based functional
unit of the product, assuming continuous recurring of functional units in the same amount every
year (Guin´ee et al., 2022). Construction projects are usually just constructed once and not being
continuously produced or consumed as regular products. The division in this report using the life-
time is therefore assessed to be reasonable.
There are several uncertainties tied to the lifetime of the project, and the lifetime is also dependent
on the maintenance of the system, which is not included in the scope in this report, and the ’cultural
lifetime’ mentioned by Sigurd Hoffmann (Hoffman and Werther, 2024). The graph in Figure 17 of
the absolute sustainability of KLAR Forsyning shows the relation between the lifetime and the
sustainability ratio SR. It follows a decreasing power function, meaning that it decreases fast in the
beginning but flattens out with longer lifetimes. This suggests that even though SR is dependent
on lifetime, a change in lifetime from 40 to 50 years does not affect the SR as much as a change
from 1 to 5 years. For drainage projects, they often have a very long lifetime, sometimes more than
a 100 years. Uncertainties in lifetimes between 50 and 100 years are therefore assessed to be less
significant than if the projects were expected to have shorter lifetimes generally.
8.10
Burden Shifting
As mentioned in Section 2.1, one of the strengths of an LCA is the life cycle perspective which
is adopted in order not to risk burden shifting. In this report, the focus has only been on cli-
mate change, and partly on resource use, but no other environmental impact category has been in
the scope. Version 3.1 of InfraLCA has not fully updated all the data to align with the standard
EN15804:2012+A2:2019, and it is therefore only possible to calculate the carbon footprint (Vejdi-
75
==PAGE:86==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
rektoratet, n.d.). Furthermore, one of the objectives in the screening tool was to make it simple to
use and understand, also for non-LCA experts. Limiting the amount of outputs helps this objective,
but this should not be an excuse for leaving out important considerations of burden shifting. If it
becomes possible to calculate all environmental impacts using the screening tool and this is added,
it is imperative to communicate the results in an understandable way to users as to not overwhelm
them with information. Many of the environmental impacts can seem foreign and complex and
requires explanation. It might be beneficial with a small guide of how to navigate and potentially
weigh the different environmental impacts. But this has not been within the scope of this report,
and is therefore just noted here as a point of attention and an idea for future improvement.
8.11
Implementation of the Screening Tool in the Utility Sector
One thing is designing and testing a screening tool, another is to get it out into the world and live.
This step has not been implemented directly in this report, but several considerations have been
put in the design of the screening tool in order to make it accessible. The choice of building upon
the existing, and freely available tool InfraLCA, ensures recognisability among people within the
sector. InfraLCA is Excel-based, and Excel is a well-known program in basically all organisations.
Furthermore, the main data source in InfraLCA is EPD-based.
EPDs are freely available, and
the data is therefore also free, leading to the possibilities of free distribution. Even so, it does re-
quire that someone updates the data in InfraLCA frequently, as EPDs usually expire after five years.
It can be difficult to prioritise the most important things to optimise or change in a project. The
screening tool can offer some obvious hot-spots to act upon. This is useful, but it should not take
away the focus for other potential improvements. In the sensitivity analysis from Case 3, Section
7.4, only a 4% change in the total impact was seen when decreasing the traffic class for concrete
paving stones from T2 to T0 and the gravel path from T1 to T0. How do we determine if those few
percent are worth considering? When deciding between different solutions, several criteria, such
as economy and customer preferences, are considered, as mentioned in Section 2.1.1. Decreasing
the traffic class, and thereby the thickness of concrete paving stones, decreases the amount needed,
which also leads to cost reductions. A small optimisation in CO2-eq emissions can therefore have a
positive significant impact when aggregating effects of other criteria. A screening tool for assessing
carbon emissions should therefore be seen as a supplement in the decision-making process, as it can
inspire to identify different optimisations and changes.
In the interview with Jacob from VandCenter Syd, he mentioned the challenges with how to weigh
sustainability in the decision-making process (Damkjær, 2025). The screening tool is just one pa-
rameter in the decision-process, and it is up to each decision-maker how the results ought to be
weighted up against other parameters. The study from Subal et al. (2024) reflected how different
factors affect the degree of implementation of LCA in decision-making, see Section 2.1.1. These
factors include complex LCA methods and results, lack of data and competent personnel, difficul-
ties in communicating LCA results, and high costs. We cannot solve all of these aspects in this
report, but several of the factors have been considered in the design process of the screening tool.
Building upon the methodology in InfraLCA, extra steps have been taken towards harmonising
LCA methods within the construction sector. Since the main result in the screening tool is based
on our assumptions and calculations, lack of data should be a minor issue, as the requirements for
data input are low. The data input does not require LCA background, but the results still require
some understanding of LCA terms. A comprehensive LCA requires a lot of data and time, which
often results in high costs. The finished screening tool itself performs an LCA quite fast with little
76
==PAGE:87==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
input, and is therefore quite low-cost in terms of the use. Even so, the version of the screening tool
that is described in this report would benefit from upgrades in regard to the coding and detail in
the assumptions and calculations. If there is a need for using it on other types of projects, than the
ones it offers now, new assumptions, inputs, and calculations also need to be made. This requires
time, data, and effort, which in turn risks resulting in high cost. Whether the final costs that are
saved during its use can make up for the costs required for its development and maintenance, would
require a cost-benefit analysis, which is not within the scope of this report.
The screening tool has been made with mainly the utility sector and relevant authorities in mind,
and Jacob mentioned that potential of the screening tool is high, since many utility companies are
in need of ways to perform LCA calculations Damkjær (2025). These stakeholders are the ones
planning and implementing drainage and climate change adaptation projects. But could it also be
used elsewhere? The tool is mainly based on premises that are specific to Danish conditions. Some
of the basic calculations, e.g. soil excavated from a pipe trench, might be transferable to other
countries, but generally the inputs and data build upon Danish regulations and emission factors
based on materials used in the Danish market. Translating this to other contexts would require
several modifications.
77
==PAGE:88==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
9
Conclusion
This report has investigated how LCA can be used early in the project period in decision-making in
construction projects in the utility sector. Currently, the utility sector and other relevant stakehold-
ers work with sustainability in different ways, including making qualitative assessments of different
environmental impact categories, sharing of experience with sustainability through meetings, adding
sustainability to the vision and strategy of the organisation, and using different tools. LCA was
used sometimes, but not widely implemented in the sector. The main challenges of using LCA in
decision-making contexts, relate to complex LCA methods and results, lack of data and competent
personnel, difficulties in communicating and assessing LCA results, and high costs. There is cur-
rently not one standard way of performing LCAs within the utility sector, though this is one of the
main goals of the tool ForsyningsLCA, which is currently in development. The advantage of using
InfraLCA to build the screening tool upon, is that it already offers a degree of harmonisation by
using the same method as used in other construction projects related to infrastructure.
In the screening tool, we made several assumptions and calculations in order to limit the amount of
input data needed from the user. The cases were used to identify which data inputs that contribute
significantly to the final result and therefore are important to have as possible user inputs. The
main takeaways are that the amount of large and/or heavy components like pipes, manholes, and
sewer overflows, and the type of material, they consist of, are very relevant to be able to change.
The screening tool assumes the amount of soil and gravel used in the project, and this was found
to be overestimated. It is therefore relevant to further investigate, if the correct assumptions are
applied in the screening tool. The precise amount of gravel and soil are often not known to the
user during screening, and making it a direct user input might therefore not be practical. The main
contribution from both soil and gravel is from their transportation, which is also affected by the
amount. An option could therefore be for the user to be able to choose a specific distance, or by
making predefined optimisation options of decreased transport or amount of soil and gravel in their
project.
In regard to road construction, the amount of materials is important, but this is based on cate-
gorisations of traffic class and frost susceptibility, and it is therefore not clear to the user exactly
how much is used in the different layers. This could potentially be made more transparent. Dis-
crepancies between actual amounts of concrete pavement stones and the amount in the screening
tool based on the traffic class was found in Case 3, as well as the amount of subbase gravel used
in road construction which is underestimated in the screening tool. It would therefore be beneficial
to look more into the relation between frost susceptibility, traffic class, and thickness of subbase
gravel and pavements. Furthermore, the material SMA was found to account for half the emissions
from asphalt roads in Case 2, and this is therefore relevant to include in future versions of the
screening tool. Transport distances of small to medium plastic and concrete pipes, manholes, and
sewer overflows are less sensitive, and can therefore be predefined by the tool. This also includes
materials like asphalt, and the shorter transport distances of soil on site to and from a depot. Plant
disposal and road reparation were only investigated in Case 2 and for now are found to be negligible
in terms of screening purposes.
Everything has an impact, and the main focus in this report has been on where to cut off in order
to require as little input as possible. This is a difficult balance to strike, and the choices in this
report have been based on elements with most impact. But a way of circumventing this is, as also
done in our screening tool, to present the user with predefined optimisation options. This way, rel-
78
==PAGE:89==
Master Thesis
Jan 2025
ative changes can still be captured without the need of specific inputs from the user. Optimisation
options included in this version of the screening tool are the use of electric machines and excavators
on site, reused gravel, and more sustainable concrete and plastic materials for the pipes, but several
other options could very well be added.
It is also important to acknowledge the limitations and scope of a screening tool. It is not meant to
give the full truth of the environmental impacts of a project, but rather to facilitate a fast and easy
way to compare several solutions of a construction project. More detailed optimisations on specific
materials and decreasing the transport distance of manholes can be made later in the project phase,
and it might be useful to have other tools, e.g. the standard version of InfraLCA, where the user
can change all those different parameters themselves.
A key focus was also to make the tool user-friendly and accessible to non-LCA experts. This was
done by decreasing the amount of input needed, adding explanation boxes, and testing the formula-
tions on relevant stakeholders in order to correct unclear formulations. Generally, it was found to be
very important to give clear instructions and descriptions of the input and the result, but also make
the underlying assumptions available and transparent. This increases the trust in the results and
the comparability across several users, as the understanding and use of the screening tool are aligned.
The communication of the results were made through different graphs in the screening tool. The
sector often mainly focuses on the production, transport, and installation, equivalent to stages A1-
A5, and therefore the first figure is a bar chart of the impacts from these stages. This bar chart is
split into predefined categories, that are more easily understood, like pavement and soil handling.
It is possible to add up to three scenarios, displayed on a bar chart for comparison. Resource use
was mentioned as another important factor to stakeholders in the industry, and a bar chart was
therefore also included of the resource use in terms of tonnes gravel. In order to relate how much a
kg of CO2 actually is, the total impact of the projects was also given in other carbon equivalents,
like kilos of beef or number of flights to New Work from Denmark.
Finally, a chart of the absolute sustainability of the project was also added to the result page.
Two different allocation methods were used, economic allocation and a version of grandfathering,
in order to estimate the sustainability ratio. This gives an indication of how well a project performs
in terms of the planetary boundary for climate change.
When testing it on Case 2 and 3, the
sustainability ratio ranged between 0.3-1.4. These construction projects are therefore deemed as
absolute sustainable or just above the threshold of absolute sustainability, using these allocation
methods. The concept of absolute sustainability is still quite new and requires more explanation
than what is currently offered in the screening tool. But it is a first attempt to include threshold
thinking into impact assessment, raising it above a mere comparison of solutions.
We have given our proposal of how to perform a quick LCA of construction projects in the utility
sector through a screening tool with little data input and predefined categories, that can be used
in the early stage of the project period. Thought has been put into the formulation of categories
and input, in order to make it accessible to decision-makers, and several solutions can be compared
in the tool. The tool includes suggestions for optimisations in order to provoke decision-makers to
include those in their considerations. So what is the conclusion on the carbon cost of cobblestones,
clinkers, and concrete? Try our screening tool and find out today!

11.2

11.2.4
Calculation of Manholes
The weight of the concrete manholes are assumed based on their dimensions. The manufacturer,
IBF, has a selection of standard manholes consisting of a bottom, a middle ring and a top, which all
come in different heights. Depending on the wanted height of 1.5, 2.5, or 3.5 meters, the estimated
manhole-element weights are found in the manhole product catalogue, and added together (IBF,
n.d.b). The total weights of the manholes can be found in Table 16.
Table 16: Total weight of concrete manholes dependent of their inner diameter.
Weight [kg]
Diameter [mm]
1.5 m
2.5 m
3.5 m
600
1000
1265
1795
1000
1760
2325
3065
1250
2700
3200
4130
1500
5100
7550
8500
2000
7730
9600
12100
2500
11600
14000
16400
3000
16700
19900
23000
The volume for excavation for the manholes is calculated as a box, where two sides have an exca-
vation of 0.5 m besides the diameter of the pipe. The excavated volume is calculated as follows:
V = (d + 0.5 · 2) · d · h
(7)
, where d is the diameter of the manhole, 0.5 is the assumed extra excavation next to the manhole,
and h is the height of the manhole. This way of calculating the volume does not take into account
the wall thickness of the manhole, which, for the higher diameter manholes, would be significant.
It does also not fit with the trapeze shape of the pipe trench for unpaved areas, which it would do
in practice. The width of the manholes are not part of the pipe trench length chosen by the user.
Information about volumes can be found in Table 17.

11.3.9
Absorption of CO2 by concrete
For the concrete pipes in the Detailed LCA, the EPD NEPD-5624-4923-DK from IBF was used
Larsen, Anderson, Mølgaard and Hauan (2024). In this EPD, a negative emission was reported in
the use phase, B1. According to a study from Syddansk Universitet, concrete reabsorb atmospheric
CO2 during its lifetime, called the ”sponge effect”. The hydrated concrete undergoes carbonation
and forms calcium carbonate (CaCO3) (Cao et al., 2020). InfraLCA does not account for the B1,
and therefore this effect is not included in the final results. If this effect is applied manually to the
concrete pipes used in Stretch 1, the total emissions from the project decrease by 0.41% compared to
the baseline and decrease by 1.2% when only looking at the total emissions from the concrete pipes
alone (Bing et al., 2023). In the EPD of concrete manholes, this effect is not included (Larsen, Borg,
Greibe and Hauan, 2024), and the effect of the carbon uptake of these is therefore not investigated
here. But the sponge effect is general to concrete products and can be seen in other EPDs as well
(Larsen and Merlin, 2021; ?). It was estimated that from 2010 to 2020, 1.09% of the average global
land carbon sink was attributed the carbon uptake by lime. In this report, this effect will not be
accounted for as the system boundary is defined without the use phase of the projects. For screening
purposes, the effect is assumed to be negligible, but it is worth noting that emissions from concrete
materials might be slightly overestimated. For future studies, carbon sink could be a parameter to
include in the analysis.
100
